A. Project Development Objective
1. Project development objective: (see Annex 1)
The objective of the project is to secure, over the medium-term, an acceptable level of service on the
Djibouti-Galafi Road which constitutes Djibouti Port's transit corridor to Ethiopia, during the period when
a long-term corridor improvement scheme is being developed and implemented by the Ethiopian and
Djibouti governments with assistance from the European Union.
Following the outbreak of armed conflict between Ethiopia and Eritrea in 1998, Djibouti has served as the
primary transit port for landlocked Ethiopia. The port of Assab, on the Eritrean coast, formerly played this
role, first as part of Ethiopia, then under an agreement between Ethiopia and Eritrea following Eritrean
independence in 1991. The road corridor between Addis and Assab, which passes some 20 kilometers from
the Djibouti border, was upgraded and improved for this purpose. With the sudden shift of Ethiopian traffic
from Assab Port to Djibouti Port in 1998, the 20 year old road connection between Djibouti Port and the
Addis-Assab Road (via Dikhil and Galafi, see Map No. 30713 attached) received a level of heavy-vehicle
traffic it was not in a condition to carry. While the Ethiopian Road Authority (ERA) has repaired and
upgraded the 20 km spur road within Ethiopia from the Addis-Assab Road to the Djibouti border at the
town of Galafi, the 221 km highway between Djibouti Port and the Ethiopian border has steadily
deteriorated as a result of the heavy traffic and inadequate maintenance. Portions of the road are presently
disintegrating, posing a risk of complete failure on long sections. Maintaining this road link is critical to
Djibouti Port's ability to efficiently serve the Ethiopian traffic.
The project will finance a limited, but adequate, rehabilitation of the road pavement and emergency repair
of drainage facilities and ensure that these will be well maintained over the medium-term. This will
guarantee that the existing lifeline to Addis-Ababa -- through which crucial Ethiopian agricultural imports
such as fertilizers, as well as international famine-relief shipments must pass -- remains functional until a
joint Djiboutian - Ethiopian corridor development project, financed in large part by the European Union, is
realized. Even under the most optimistic of scenarios, the implementation of this EU-financed project could
only start in-the middle of 2002 and would take at least three years to complete, during which period, it is
clear that the Djibouti-Galafi road cannot remain in its present state without doing serious harm to the
economies of both Djibouti and Ethiopia. By providing a complementary, interim improvement of the
existing road, this Credit could also enhance the project to be financed by the EU, by allowing sufficient
time to consider all options for an efficient transport corridor from Djibouti Port to Addis-Ababa without
the pressures of having to react to an emergency situation.
Most importantly, the project addresses an urgent need. As a result of the conflict with Eritrea, Ethiopia's
only access to the sea is through Djibouti, and repairs to this road are now critical in view of a potential
famine in Ethiopia caused by a long period of drought. In the event of a famine, this road would be the
primary lifeline for supplying an estimated 1,000,000 tons of food aid expected to be needed in the near
future.
2. Key performance indicators: (see Annex I)
The key performance indicators would include:
Output:
*   Shortening of the travel time between Djibouti Port and the Ethiopian border by more than 50
percent;
-2-



*   Reduction in traffic accidents by 35%;
*   40% reduction in vehicle breakdowns and vehicle operating costs;
*   Elimination of the dust problems now associated with vehicular traffic on this road; and
*   Proper disposal and collection of waste, regrading material sites after use, and use of archaelogical
monitoring.
Outcome/impact:
*   Continued increase in the use of Djibouti Port, as shown by Djibouti Port statistics.
*   Substantial completion of road rehabilitation by June 30, 2001.
B. Strategic Context
1. Sector-related Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) goal supported by the project: (see Annex 1)
Document number: P-5416-DJI        Date of latest CAS discussion: 01/15/91
Country Background. Djibouti is a small country with an estimated population of 700,000, about
two-thirds of whom live in Djibouti city. The country has little physical resources. It is an arid desert with
no permanent rivers or fresh surface water, and less than 0.03% of the land area is cultivable. Agriculture
is limited to nomadic livestock raising. The fisheries sector has some potential but there has been little
commercial exploitation. The industrial sector is almost non-existent, reflecting the high cost and low
productivity of Djiboutian labor. The economy is essentially based on the port and the road and rail links
that lead from it, French financial assistance, including budgetary support, and direct and indirect spillover
from the French military presence. The port has become especially important since the start of the
Ethiopian-Eritrean war in 1998 and the switch of Ethiopia's transit traffic from Assab to Djibouti.
Reflecting mainly the narrow resource base, decline in external assistance, and slow-down in port activity
(prior to Ethiopian-Eritrean war in 1998) the economy stagnated over much of the last two decades. Per
capita GDP declined from $1500 in 1978 to $780 at present, unemployment has risen sharply and is about
44% at present. Poverty is widespread and pervasive. A 1997 Bank Poverty Assessment indicated that
approximately 45% of the population is poor, and 10% are in absolute poverty. Only one in three school
age children attends primary school, and health service delivery is very weak. The fiscal situation has also
worsened, with Government arrears amounting to almost 23% of GDP in 1998. Further, the lingering
effects of a sharp build-up in Djibouti's military expenditures (from 4% of GDP in 1989-90 to 16% in
1992-93) in response to an armed uprising in 1991-94 , have exposed the fragility of the economy and
exacerbated macro imbalances.
In an attempt to reverse the negative trends and with external support dwindling, the Government started
implementing a macro-economic adjustment over the period 1996-98 with the support of an IMF stand-by
arrangement (SBA), and is discussing with the Fund a structural reform program supported by an IMF
Poverty Reduction and Growth Facility (PRGF) during 1999-02. The Government has asked for IDA
support in implementing structural reforms.
Bank Assistance Strategy. A CAS for Djibouti is currently under preparation. Responsibility for the
country was transferred from the Bank's Africa region to MNA in October 1999. This will be the first
CAS for the country. There has not been a CAS in the past partly because of limited Bank activity in the
country. Based on discussions with the Government, lessons learned from limited past involvement in the
country, as well as the country's weak institutional capacity and limited IDA funds and Bank
administrative resources, Bank assistance during the next few years will have to be very focused on a few
areas where it can make the most difference in terms of growth and poverty reduction. The most likely
areas for Bank support are set out below:
-3-



(i) Re-establishing a more competitive and sustainable macroeconomic environment. We will work
closely with the IMF, which will take the lead on this.
(ii)      Re-starting growth by taking maximum advantage of the transit traffic to Ethiopia using the
Djibouti Port to restart growth. The port and associated services are the main avenues for growth
of the economy. In the near-term, this requires investments in urgently fixing the most deteriorated
portion of the road that links the port to Ethiopia, which should translate into immediate increase in
the capacity use of the port and a sizeable growth impact.
(iii) Addressing the problem of poverty. While specific actions will need to be explored in connection
with a planned Poverty Reduction Strategy Paper, likely areas of attention will be education--to
reverse the dramatic low enrollment rate in education; addressing the overwhelming needs in health
(AIDS and Tuberculosis crisis); and prepare a possible program of assistance for the poor to
cushion the impact of adjustment and inevitable decline in real wages anticipated.
(iv) Tuming around the performance of IDA portfolio and improving its development impact, which
are currently weak.
(v) Mobilizing resources from other donors around social development and poverty reduction.
Sector Strategy. As mentioned previously, the port and its associated service infrastructure, represent the
most significant avenue of growth for the Djibouti economy. While enough investments (mainly financed by
different Arab Funds) are on-going or are planned for the development of the port, there has not been much
attention to the road system. In order for Djibouti to reap the benefits of the increased transit traffic it must
repair and upgrade the transport corridor leading form the port to the Ethiopian border. The Ethiopians
have repaired and upgraded the spur of the Addis Ababa-Assab road to the Djibouti border. The current
famine in Ethiopia and the need to transport large quantities of food aid on the Djibouti-Ethiopia corridor
has introduced an emergency element into an already urgent situation.
2. Main sector issues and Government strategy:
Background. The Port of Djibouti and the transport of goods from the port are, with the possible exception
of official development assistance, the mainstays of the economy of Djibouti. Its strategic location on the
Red Sea and Gulf of Aden's intemational shipping routes, as well as in relation to its East African
neighbors, potentially make Djibouti an excellent entrepot for the region. For various reasons related to
geo-politics and infrastructure deficiencies, this potential has barely begun to be tapped. With
unemployment rates reportedly as high as 40 percent, development of the trade and transport sectors, which
could generate considerable employment in the local economy, is clearly a high priority for the
Govemment.
Up until less than two years ago, the Port of Djibouti was the primary transshipment location for
intemational shipping in the area. However, recent developments of new ports in the region have tended to
reduce Djibouti's role in the transshipment of international trade cargoes. In early 1998, the ports of Aden
in Yemen and Salalah in Oman opened large, modem, deepwater facilities, and major shipping lines are
now beginning to shift their transshipment business from Djibouti to those ports. Fortunately, this drop in
transshipment traffic was compensated by a shift of Ethiopian traffic in May 1998, from Assab Port in
Eritrea to Djibouti due to a further deterioration in relations between Ethiopia and Eritrea. Simultaneously,
officials at the Port of Djibouti recognized the future strategic importance of Djibouti as a transit center for
- 4 -



goods destined for countries in the Horn of Africa, and the need to focus on the transit rather than the
transshipment market. As a result, traffic overall at Djibouti has increased significantly from 1.7 million
tons in 1997 to over 4 million tons in 1998. The new traffic mix is arguably more lucrative for the country,
since it is likely to provide greater employment opportunities for the local population. While Ethiopia will
look to diversify its trade links to the outside world, Djibouti is likely to remain the predominant port for
Ethiopian goods for at least the lifetime of the proposed project. For the Djibouti government to protect that
predominant market share, it will need to ensure the quality of the entire logistics system in the long run,
from dockside to the Ethiopian border.
Road Conditions. The existing truck traffic from Djibouti to Addis Ababa utilizes the Addis Ababa -
Assab road, connecting to it through Galafi at the Djibouti/Ethiopia border (see project map attached). This
Djibouti-Galafi road was constructed in the late 1970s and was designed for mostly light traffic during the
period when Ethiopia's principal port was Assab. The road has been deteriorating steadily since 1991,
when Ethiopian troops transited along it with heavy military equipment. The subsequent closing of Assab
port in 1998, and the consequent diversion of Ethiopian traffic to Djibouti Port, has meant a steady stream
of five and six axle trucks on an already weakened and damaged road. Traffic between Ethiopia and
Djibouti Port in 1999 amounted to well over 85,000 truck round-trips, an average of 260 trucks per day in
each direction. This route includes about 221 kms within Djibouti, of which the 102 km nearest to the
border are in extremely poor shape. The bituminous surfacing has been removed, and the other pavement
layers (sub-base and base courses) are disintegrating.
Of the remaining paved section, the first 33 km from Djibouti City was recently reconstructed under an
Italian bilateral aid project. However, the pavement of the other 86 km of paved road -- from the Arta spur
road to Dikhil -- is also in a very poor condition, causing frequent breakdowns of trucks. An international
contractor has undertaken temporary maintenance works worth about US$2.8 million financed by the
French Ministry of Foreign Affairs to improve the situation on this 86 km section of the road. However,
although the funding has been fully utilized and the contractor has demobilized, there are still a number of
sections, particularly in the Petit Bara area (about 15 km), where there are remaining road failures in
urgent need of repair. The Ethiopian Roads Authority (ERA) has also provided some assistance in the past
to keep the Galafi-Dikhil road section open. The sections of the route within Ethiopia are in good condition
or are being improved under another IDA credit (Ethiopia - Road Sector Development Program Support
Project - Cr. 3032-ET) approved in FY98.
A feasibility study leading to preliminary and detailed engineering designs is to start shortly, to examine
options for long-term solutions to improve the road connection between Djibouti and Addis Ababa. The
European Commission (EC) Delegation in Addis Ababa has committed the equivalent of about US$25
million to this program of road works from EU regional funds. The alternatives being studied include a new
road from Djibouti to Addis-Ababa via Dire Dawa in Ethiopia, which would probably also require
investments of the same magnitude (see attached map). However, this program will, take about 5-6 years to
implement, including studies, detailed design, bidding, contract award and construction. In the interim, the
existing road needs to be improved to handle the heavy traffic now using the road and to reduce any risk of
interruption which may threaten the viability of the Port which is Djibouti's primary econromic asset.
The US $2.8 million provided by France for temporary works on the Arta - Dikhil portion of the existing
route is considerably less than what is needed to provide even temporary relief over the medium term (5
years). The proposed emergency repair and upgrading program would include repair works and intensive
maintenance on this 86 km section. The remaining Dikhil to Galafi portion (about 102 km), needs more
extensive rehabilitation work, including bituminous surfacing and temporary repair of damaged structures
(mostly Irish crossings) in order to assure its viability during the interim period. The total program would
- 5 -



have to be initiated relatively quickly and implementation should not take more than 9 months once a
contract has been signed. The program as designed would also generate a significant amount of local
employment.
Road Management. Since 1999, when the road function was transferred from the Public Works
Department, in the former Ministry of Public Works, to the Ministry of Equipment and Transport, there
has been a considerable reduction in qualified staff in the Roads Department. The director of the
department has been appointed on a temporary basis for more than a year, and the role and mandate of the
department has become unclear. Five engineers and two technicians have been transferred from the Roads
Department to other ministries within the Government. This loss of staff has significantly weakened the
institutional capacity of the department to operate in a satisfactory manner. The staffing changes expected
are shown in the table below:
Staffing                               March 1999       July 2000
Engineers                                    8               3
Technical Advisors (French)                  3               1
Technicians                                  7               5
Administrative, accounting, clerical, etc  420             281
The Roads Department is organized into three divisions and eight subdivisions, with a total personnel of
281. There are 3 local engineers and three French technical advisors. Two of the three advisors are leaving
in July 2000, and the advisor to the acting director is leaving in December 2000, due to a planned reduction
in French technical assistance. The equipment fleet of about 160 pieces of all types of road construction
and maintenance equipment is largely inoperable because of lack of spare parts, tires and batteries.
Until 1998 the Roads Department staff was responsible for construction and maintenance of the entire
Djibouti road network, which consists of about 1370 km of roads distributed as follows:
Type          Inter-city     Urban          Total
Paved surface  441.2*        115            556.2
Gravel        209.4           85            294.4
Track         520.9                         520.9
Total       1,171.5          200          1,371.5
*) 102 km of this total includes Dikhil-Galafi where the pavement is disintegrating and the surfacing is completely gone.
Another 15km is in danger of being damaged beyond repair in the very near future, while another 55 km is at risk.
After the reshuffle of ministries in 1999, the responsibility of the Roads Department has been reduced to
that of managing the national (inter-city) roads only.
The available road budget for the last three years has been on the order of DJF 250 million (about US$1.4
million) and diminishing every year. More than half of the budget is spent on salaries. The roads
department is generally unable to spend the rest of the allocated funds -- for road maintenance, road
investments, and upkeep of equipment fleet -- because of a lack of qualified staff, unavailability of spare
parts and because the budgeted amounts are not fully released by the Ministry of Finance. The civil service
salaries for January to April 2000 have not yet been paid, and the salaries for December 1999 were paid at
the end of April, 4 months late. Despite obvious needs, this situation has contributed to a weakening of the
sector over the last several years.
-6 -



Road Sector Development.. The Ministry has drawn up an ambitious program of road sector development
initiatives which include: cost recovery to fund road maintenance - primarily through a tariff on trucks
leaving the port; axle load controls through construction of weigh bridges; and a restructuring of road
sector institutions in line with the Bank's recommendations under the RMI (Road Management Initiative).
Institutional Support would be critical to the sustainability of investments in the road sector, and the project
would supplement the EU's financing of technical assistance. A review of the management and financing of
the road sector is provided in Annex 16 which also identifies the key issues needing to be addressed to
achieve sustainability in road sector operations.
Transport Corridor Development. Long term options for the development of the Djibouti-Addis corridor
are currently under review by various assistance agencies, including the Agence Francaise and the
European Union. There are several corridor development strategies which are not mutually exclusive. One
is to upgrade the existing rail connection, the Chemin de Fer Djibouti - Ethiopian (CDE) between Djibouti
Port and Addis Ababa via the Ethiopian town of Dire Dawa. Since most of the traffic is either
containerized (most already arrives at the port containerized), or bulk commodities (dry/liquid), and the
origin-destination is of a point-to-point nature (Port to Addis), rail seems to be a reasonable option. A
second option is to construct a high quality road link along the same route as the rail link -- via Ali Sabieh
in Djibouti and Dire Dawa. Finally, the existing route, via Dikhil and Galafi, could be substantially
upgraded and re-built. Clearly, the EU-financed corridor development project will need to take such
strategic considerations into account.
Whatever strategy is adopted, the corridor development program requires a clearly specified and phased set
of transport development actions. These should encompass both physical infrastructure and institutional
development spanning the port, road, trucking and rail sectors. The program must be integrated with the
transportation planning of all the affected countries in the region, ensuring that important aspects of
transport policy, such as regulations (e.g. axle load limits, through bills of lading, etc.) are harmonized.
Presently, there is a joint committee established between Djibouti and Ethiopia for this purpose, and it is
critical that the Bank and other multi- and bi-lateral aid agencies support this close cooperation with a
coordinated strategy. Internally, the Bank has established close cooperation between the Ethiopia transport
team and the team responsible for this project. The long-term development of a Djibouti-Addis corridor is
an important component in the development of the Djiboutian economy. It would provide Djiboutians with
a sustainable development path focussing on a wide range of employment opportunities from port and rail
services, trucking and freight forwarding, to banking, hotel and entertainment services. Presently,
Djiboutian involvement in the trucking industry is minimal, with more than 90 percent of the cargo being
carried by Ethiopian truckers. A small TA component is included in this project to assist the Djibouti
Government formulate a strategy for development of these trade-related services.
-7 -



3. Sector issues to be addressed by the project and strategic choices:
Due to the urgent nature of the project and the short duration of the civil works, it is not possible to fully
address all the considerable policy, institutional and human resource development issues related to the road
sector during project preparation or the early stages of implementation. Therefore, in order to expedite the
project, the preparation and implementation has been staged in two phases. During the first 12 months of
the Project (Phase I), in parallel with the urgent road rehabilitation works, there will be a need to undertake
institutional studies and policy development activities related to the sustainability of the road sector.
During the subsequent 3 years of the Project (O&M, Phase II) the focus would be on implementing the
outcomes of the Phase I policy and institutional studies to be discussed and agreed at an Early
Implementation Assessment carried out towards the end of Phase I. A letter of sector policy (framework
agreement) outlining the actions to be taken by the Government in this respect was agreed at Negotiations,
and a signed copy was received on May 31, 2000, and is attached as Annex 11.
C. Project Description Summary
1. Project components (see Annex 2 for a detailed description and Annex 3 for a detailed cost
breakdown):
The project components can be separated into two groups depending on the implementation phase: in the
initial phase, urgent, quick rehabilitation and repairs of the road between the Arta turnoff and Galafi, on the
Ethiopian border over a period of about one year; and a second three-year phase covering maintenance of
the road, construction of axle load control stations, and technical assistance (TA) to support, institutional
development in various aspects of road maintenance, the transport sector and port/trade related economic
development. Phase I would have the following main sub-components: (a) a low cost temporary
improvement of the National Road #1, between Dikhil and Galafi (102 km); (b) repairs of National Road
#1, between the turnoff to Arta and Dikhil (86 km); and (c) procurement of spare parts to make operational
the usable parts of the Road Department's plant pool. Phase II would have the following components: (a)
routine maintenance of Arta to Dikhil and Dikhil to Galafi sections of National Road #1 for a period of 3
years, after the improvement and repair works have been completed; and (b) construction of axle load
control stations and procurement of related equipment. The TA components will provide services to
support the establishment of a Project Implementation Unit (PIU), including training for PIU staff, support
for institutional changes in the sector, facilitate the development of trade related services in Djibouti,
undertake an HIV/AIDS outreach program for truckers and construction workers and a road safety study,
and assist the Government in undertaking the project within a very short time frame. If practical and with
the understanding of the EC Delegation and the Government, the project would also implement agreed
policy and institutional recommendations of the EU financed study. The table below summarizes these
components, their costs and the allocation of the credit prodeeds.

2. Key policy and institutional reforms supported by the project:
The Project would include the undertaking of agreed studies, policy planning workshops and other
necessary actions during the first 12 months of the project implementation (Phase I) to facilitate a process
of institutional transformation and achieve financial sustainability of the road sector. The future role of
force account operations in the road sector would form part of the studies. Outcomes of the EU-financed
studies on road maintenance by contract would be an important element in this process. At an Early
Implementation Assessment, agreement would be reached on the specifics of the key policy and institutional
reforms, and this would be followed by a three year action program to introduce effective institutional
arrangements and full off-budget financing of the road sector (Phase 11 of the Project). A framework for
the future direction of the road sector is set out in the Letter of Road Sector Policy (Annex 11), and details
of actions to be taken during the first 12 months of the Project are in the TOR for Institutional
Development Program (Annex 13).

4. Institutional and implementation arrangements:
Implementation Period. As explained above, in order to expedite processing, the project would be
prepared and implemented in two phases, Phase I being the road rehabilitation works (about I year) and
Phase II being institution building and operation and maintenance (about 3 years), with some of the
preparation of the second phase being carried out during implementation of the first. In developing the
institution building component, the experience under the previous IDA financed project (See below -
Lessons Learned) would be taken into account, and a detailed plan for this component would be agreed at
- 1 0 -



an Early Implementation Assessment carried out towards the end of the first phase, i.e. about a year from
project effectiveness.
Institutional Arrangements. The project would be implemented by a Project Implementation Unit (PIU)
established within the Ministry of Equipment and Transport. This unit would be delegated full authority by
the Government to make efficient managerial decisions and to authorize all payments under the Project.
The PIU would sign all civil works contracts, goods contracts and technical assistance contracts, and
operate the Financial Management System and the Special Account for the project. The Project Director,
in charge of the PIU, should have sufficient rank and authority to obtain quick decisions from the country's
top decision makers and should have the capacity to execute decisions rapidly. A consulting firm would
assist the PIU and be responsible for all construction management. This Construction Manager, recruited
internationally, would be based in Dikhil, at the Roads Department's workshop and road camp. The
Construction Manager would oversee and coordinate the work of the various subcontractors, as well as a
Force Account unit which would be assigned to the Construction Manager for the life of the construction
period. The Construction Manager will have direct responsibility for organizing and directing the work of
this Force Account unit. The various construction contractors - currently, three are envisioned - would
be coordinated and monitored by the Construction Manager, but would sign contracts directly with the
Project Implementation Unit.
Control of quality of works undertaken by the Force Account Unit and local sub-contractors would be the
responsibility of an independent Resident Supervision Engineer, to be recruited internationally. Because of
the urgency of the situation, it may be desirable to begin some activities related to implementation -- such
as production of aggregate - early, and this would be explored by project preparation consultants who
would also prepare bidding documents prior to the appointment of the Construction Manager. The Terms
of Reference of the consultants, Construction Manager and the Supervision Engineer are attached as Annex
13.
The above implementation arrangements are critical for a successful, fast track completion of the physical
components of the project. IDA no-objection to the shortlist of consulting firms and the 'request for
proposal' package was given at Negotiations, and appointment of the Construction Manager and the
Supervision Engineer is a condition of credit effectiveness.
Financial Management. The Project will be implemented by a PIU, which will be in charge of project
financial management, including all accounting functions and reporting activities. The PIU will include an
Accountant who will report to the PIU's Project Director. The appointment of the Accountant will be a
Condition of Effectiveness. Immediately after Negotiations, a consultant will begin working with the
Ministry of Equipment and Transport to establish a project financial management system, which will
include documented accounting policies/procedures and the budgeting process, a manual accounting
system, internal controls and reporting tools to be used by the PIU. A Bank-executed Japanese Policy and
Human Resource Development Fund (PHRD) Grant could be available to fund the services of the
consultant. As an interim mechanism, and to avoid delaying Credit Effectiveness, the Project will utilize a
manual accounting system until a computerized system is tested and fully operational, which is expected to
be about five months after effectiveness. Prior to effectiveness, a Financial Management Specialist, or the
external auditor under the supervision of the Financial Management Specialist, will certify that the
financial management system is satisfactory. The establishment of a project financial management system,
including a manual accounting system, internal control mechanisms and documented operational policies
and procedures, as detailed in the action plan, is a Condition of Credit Effectiveness. A work plan for
developing appropriate accounting systems and procedures is included in Annex 5. The work plan was
agreed at Negotiations.
- 11 -



A Special Account will be opened at the Banque pour le Commerce et l'Industrie and managed by the PIU.
Signatories on the Special Account will include the PIU Director and the Minister of Finance All
transactions would be reviewed and approved by the Accountant and Project Director.
Project accounts, including the Special Account and SOEs, will be subject to external auditing by a
qualified auditor acceptable to IDA and under agreed terms of reference. The PIU will submit annual
audited financial statements to IDA within three months following the end of the fiscal year; the first such
report will be due March 31, 2001. The appointment of a qualified auditor is a Condition of Effectiveness,
and a shortlist of qualified auditors was agreed between the Borrower and IDA at Negotiations. The
auditor's terms of reference will include the submission of a "management letter" regarding the PIU's
internal controls and financial management system and procedures no later than six months after
effectiveness.
Since construction of the project roads is expected to last one year only, the Borrower opted to use
traditional methods of disbursement, which include SOEs. Replenishment of the Special Account will be
based on eligible expenditures presented in the SOE. While PMR-based disbursements will not be used for
this project, the borrower agreed to submit quarterly transitional PMRs based on the Cash Model found in
Annex 7 of the Bank's "Project Financial Management Manual" (1-A, 3-A, 3-B, 3-C, 3-D) beginning
October 31, 2000, and full PMRs beginning December 31, 2001.
D. Pro ect Rationale
1. Project alternatives considered and reasons for rejection:
Given the advanced stage of deterioration on the present roadway, creating a situation of extreme urgency,
no real alternatives to the low-cost, temporary restoration works have been considered. Many options which
are quite viable in the long run, such as an alternative alignment or upgrading the rail connection (CDE),
would be unable to meet the project objective of keeping the land connection to the port open in the near
future. However, alternative implementation options were considered in view of the need for a speedy
implementation. These are: (i) a conventional ICB contracting procedure; (ii) a design-build contracting of
the works including a five year defects liability (maintenance) period of the contractor; (iii) several small
contracts bid locally under NCB; and (iv) undertaking the works using a Management Contract and local
subcontracting, using possibly also the equipment and staff of the Roads Department to undertake some
works including subsequent maintenance.
The construction industry in Djibouti consists of small contractors, who have not executed a single contract
in the amount estimated for the emergency repairs of the Arta - Galafi road. Annex 15 provides a detailed
assessment of the Local Construction Industry. A typical large contract has been in the range of US$ 2 to 3
million by the largest contractors. Two international contractors are resident, in a small way, but they
require two months to marshal additional equipment resources necessary for implementation of all civil
works under one contract. In addition, the Roads Department has not managed a project of this size, and its
technical staff is limited in numbers and experience. All of these factors must be taken into account when
selecting the quickest and most effective way to start and complete this emergency project. For these
reasons, option (iii) was rejected, although a major consideration is to maximize the use of local resources,
including people and equipment. Option (ii), design-build, required the most front end time for definition by
the client, preparation by the bidders, evaluation and award. This would also be a major innovation as far
as the MOET is concerned, and could prove counterproductive in an emergency situation. The earliest field
work envisioned would be the first quarter of 2001 under this format, with little control of speed of
- 12 -



execution. Of the two viable options remaining, the conventional ICB was rejected for two reasons: (a) the
contract work would not be initiated before the end of the year due to the time required to tender, award and
mobilize on an ICB basis and; (b) this drastically would minimize the use of the local Djiboutian
construction industry and thereby not create the local capacity necessary for future maintenance of the
road. There is no one single contractor located in Djibouti who could execute the works with existing
resources. In effect all contractors would be forced to mobilize equipment from outside Djibouti to
undertake the whole contract within a short time period, an extra mobilization time estimated to be at least
two months.
The option selected is the construction management contract. A Construction Manager would be selected
following international competition among a shortlist of firms according to Bank Guidelines (Annex 6A).
The manager would be responsible for determining the number and combination of works and goods
contracts, plus force account contracts, that would best ensure completion of the work according to
schedule and budget. The work could be started as early as July, 2000 when it is envisioned the
management contract will be awarded. This arrangement would maximize the use of small local
contractors, existing forces of the Roads Department and local employment. It also provides professional
management expertise in Djibouti where a road project of this size and delivery requirements has not been
experienced by local contractors or the Roads Department. The construction management contract would
include the responsibility to train local Roads Department technicians and workers as a force account unit
performing construction contract work and maintenance work. A Djiboutian counterpart engineer located
in the PIU would work with the force account management unit to assume full leadership of the unit
subsequent to the construction manager's departure. Another counterpart engineer, also administratively
located in the PIU, would work with the Construction Manager on a regular basis to learn about all aspects
of contract management and control. These counterpart engineers would be selected among qualified local
engineers following an open selection process.

3. Lessons learned and reflected in the project design:
The only past Bank operation in the transport sector in Djibouti was the Highway Maintenance Project
(Credit 1303-DJI), approved by the Board on November 30, 1982 and closed on June 30, 1987. The
Project Completion Report for this project is Report No. 9111 dated September 19, 1990. The project
objective was to assist the Government in strengthening its road organization to carry out efficient road
improvement and maintenance operations. The project financed; (i) technical assistance to develop the
Roads Department; (ii) a workshop, equipment, and spare parts; and (iii) financing of materials for
implementation of a road improvement and maintenance program by force account. The main lessons
learned were: (i) that labor intensive methods are not practical and competitive with machine based
operations due to the rocky terrain and high labor costs; (ii) institutional development requires more time
and effort than was foreseen; (iii) equipment procurement was very slow due to inexperience with IDA
guidelines; and (iv) supervision efforts by the Bank could have been better and could have partly rectified
some of the implementation problems. All of these lessons, and the current state of the road network and
the public works plant pool, suggest that successful institutional development requires continuity and
long-tern engagement of aid agencies in providing technical assistance support.
The above lessons have partly been reflected in the project concept within the constraints set by the urgency
of this operation. As part of preparation, the team has reviewed several implementation options to ensure
timely completion of the works in the first year of the project. As a by-product of the chosen
implementation strategy, the force account unit of the Roads Department would be trained and developed.
The present weak capacity of the Roads Department was a major factor in selecting the construction
management method for implementation of this project.
Labor intensive methods have been considered, but given the general lack of casual labor in the project
area, the relatively high daily wage rate (US$ 10-12) in Djibouti, and the urgency of this operation, labor
intensive methods (for example, spreading the surface dressing chippings by hand) would not be
economical and would likely slow down project implementation significantly, jeopardizing the overall
project objective. The selection of the construction management method gives the maximum opportunity for
employment of local workers, consistent with the requirement to complete the project in the shortest time
possible. This will allow the use of small contracts using local contractors and the force account unit of the
Ministry of Equipment and Transport.
The state of the public plant pool, for which the last Bank operation provided funding, has been reviewed
by a consultant, and the current status of the equipment is provided in Annex 15. A small component has
been added to the Project for rehabilitation of some of this equipment. The usable equipment has been
identified along with the cost of repairs to make it operational. The best of this usable equipment will be
identified and selected for use by the force account unit in implementation of the Project. The equipment
that can be made operational, but is not needed by the force account unit, would be evaluated for future
disposal if so desired by the Government.
The supervision efforts by the Bank would be intensive in the initial stages of the project, but to a certain
- 15 -



extent, staff of the World Bank Resident Mission in Yemen could be utilized which can easily reach
Djibouti by regularly scheduled flights. Continuity can be ensured by Bank staffs close interaction with
the EU to ensure that long-term institutional support is provided also under their follow-up project.
4. Indications of borrower commitment and ownership:
The staff of the Ministry of Equipment and Transport has participated actively with the IDA identification
and preparation missions, and the Minister of Finance's recent request to the Bank for financial assistance
are good indicators of a strong commitment and willingness to address the problems facing this critical
transport corridor. Correspondence from the MOF confirming the Government's commitment to the project
was received March 26, 2000. Key messages and faxes received from other agencies involved in the
transport sector in Djibouti are available on file, showing the close coordination with the EC Delegation in
Djibouti and Addis Ababa and the World Food Program. The US Embassies in Djibouti and Ethiopia and
the French Development Agency have also been informed about the Project, and coordination meetings
have taken place with the World Food Program's representatives in Addis Ababa and Djibouti.
5. Value added of Bank support in this project:
The primary value added of the Bank's financial support is the reduction of the risk that the roadway would
become impassable for a long period of time, threatening not only the economic life of Djibouti, Ethiopian
importers and the primacy of Djibouti Port as a transit hub, but also the ability of international food aid to
reach recipients in Ethiopia in the medium term. The Bank has also considerable experience in dealing with
post conflict emergencies and addressing logistic problems in this respect. An indirect benefit of the Bank's
participation is that it would enhance the proposed corridor development project to be financed by the EU.
Also, the Bank's work in the road sector in Ethiopia permits enhanced coordination of road sector programs
in the two countries.
E. Summary Project Analysis (Detailed assessments are in the project file, see Annex 8)
1. Economic (see Annex 4):
3 Cost benefit    NPV=US$8.7 million; ERR = 31 % (see Annex 4)
O Cost effectiveness
O Other (specify)
The benefits from reductions in vehicle operating costs and travel times accrue immediately to Ethiopian
truckers. However, the competitive advantage of the Djibouti corridor with respect to other potential ports
in the region -- including, eventually, Assab -- depends on both the efficiency of the port and its links to
neighboring countries. The poor condition of the road thus constitutes a deficiency which could hinder
Djibouti's ability to compete when the geo-politics of the region once again allow for such competition.
Improving the service performance of the roadway, therefore, ultimately benefits Djibouti, by strengthening
and consolidating its competitive position. The economic analysis, therefore, uses the reduction in vehicle
operating costs (VOC) resulting from the improved road condition as a proxy for the strengthened
competitive position of the Port, since it is the amount shippers would need to receive to make them at least
economically indifferent to using another port to which road conditions are reasonably good. Reduction in
risk of road failure can also be identified as an economic benefit to Djibouti, although quantifying such a
benefit is difficult. Details of the analysis are in Annex 4 and the key results are summarized below.
Using VOC reduction as a proxy for the competitive position of the Port, three alternatives were assessed.
The proposed project rehabilitation was assessed against: (a) a scenario in which the present maintenance
- 16 -



strategy of minimal expenditures is continued and the condition of the road remains as is (Scenario A); (b)
a scenario of providing five years of continuous grading and periodic regravelling of the unpaved section
between Dikhil and Galafi (Scenario B); (c) and an optimistic scenario assuming the EU project could be
completed in three years, rather than five, with continuous grading and periodic regravelling to maintain the
road during these three years. Each of these scenarios was evaluated against a high-traffic-growth and a
no-traffic-growth assumption, reflecting significant uncertainties in the geo-politics of the region. IRRs
were calculated on the additional stream of costs and benefits of the rehabilitation project over those of the
two continuous grading scenarios. Sensitivity analysis has also been carried out, to determine: (i) the
effects of potential delays to project implementation; and (ii) the effect of a change in the value of time
savings. The results, as shown in Annex 4, show that the project remains economically worthwhile even if
there were a six month delay in implementation - except for the low-growth scenario under which the
EU-financed project is fully implemented by 2003 - but the results also show significant loss of economic
value associated with such a delay.
The calculated IRRs from these scenarios were:
Rehabilitate versus   High Growth  Low Growth   Low Growth
& 6-mth delay
A. Continuous grading   48 %         31 %          15%
5 years
B. Continuous grading:
3 years        27 %           13 %          -8%
C. Minimal Action     135%           103%          71%
Scenario A: assumes EU-financed project will take 5 years to implement
Scenario B: assumes EU-financed project will take 3 years to implement
Scenario C: assumes present minimal maintenance strategy continues
It is considered that the factors leading to the negative 8% IRR, i.e. low traffic growth, EU project
completed in three years, and a six month delay in implementation are highly unlikely. The high IRRs of
the other scenarios are common for road improvement projects involving upgrading from gravel to a low
cost paved standard, the benefits of which are significant with a high volume of truck traffic.
Regarding the specific allocation of the costs and benefits under agreed arrangements, much of the
reduction in vehicle operating costs would immediately accrue to Ethiopian trucking operations, which are
organized into five private companies, five trucking associations, five state enterprises, and numerous,
smaller own-account transporters, particularly for petroleum products. The Ethiopian and Djibouti
govemments have recently agreed to allow charges on the trucks of US$0.50 per ton (this agreement will be
reviewed as part of the Early Implementation Assessment - see Annex 16), which amounts to about $15 per
truck leaving Djibouti Port bound for Ethiopia. In the long run, this revenue level should be monitored to
determine whether it is sufficient for the needs of the corridor; preliminary information suggests that this
tariff is somewhat below accepted per ton-kilometer norms in Africa. Without detailed information on what
the more permanent transport corridor will look like, however, it would be difficult to expect the two
governments to re-open this question. Consequently, the question of road tariff levels should not be allowed
to delay the proposed credit, particularly in light of a potential famine in Ethiopia. Technical assistance on
institutional arrangements in the Djibouti transport sector, examination of the constraints facing the
development of the Djibouti trucking industry, and separate Bank non-lending assistance to Djibouti Port
- 17 -



advising on tariffs, however, are expected to address some of these issues separately.
2. Financial (see Annex 5):
NPV=US$ million; FRR = % (see Annex 4)
The Government of Djibouti has established a Road Maintenance Fund (RMF), and from February 1,
2000, the RMF started collecting user charges from trucks transiting Djibouti. Recent agreement between
Djibouti and Ethiopia has established a charge of US$0.50 per tonne of goods from Djibouti Port to
Ethiopia. While a number of technical details remain to be worked out between the two governments,
anticipated revenues from the program (estimated at about $1.4 million per year) would provide sufficient
funds to maintain the roadway over the near terrm. Government officials indicated that the RMF in its
present form is temporary in nature, and that it is intended in the longer term to have a Road Fund which is
national in scope, rather than one based solely on the transit corridor. In addition, there has been some
discussion in the Government about setting up a separate fund to handle the needs of the urban street
network. These issues will need to be clarified through the institutional technical assistance program.
Fiscal Impact:
The project has been designed to minimize its fiscal impact on government finances. The RMF is expected
to finance the necessary routine maintenance contracts to be awarded under this Project's Phase II,
providing the necessary counterpart funding for recurrent costs without affecting the general budget. Due
to the emergency nature of Phase I of this operation and the current fiscal situation, as evidenced by the
non-payment of civil service salaries (backlog of 4-5 months salaries), the project has been designed to
allow the government contribution to the civil works costs to be provided through the provision of force
account road construction equipment, as well as staff costs (altogether about US$ 2 million for a 30 km
road section planned to be implemented through force account). In this way, the uncertainties of
counterpart funds for the project is not expected to affect the urgent implementation of the corridor repair
works.
Financial Management Issues. The main financial management risk is that the development of a fully
computerized financial management system with adequate procedures and staffing at the PIU will delay
Credit Effectiveness and project implementation. To mitigate this risk, the PIU will use a manual
accounting system with adequate intemal control mechanisms, as detailed in the work plan (see Annex 5),
until a computerized system is developed and operational, which is expected to be about five months after
effectiveness. The establishment of a manual accounting system with adequate intemal control mechanisms
and documented operational policies and procedures is a Condition of Effectiveness. Implementation of the
financial management work plan will necessitate close supervision starting immediately after Negotiations.
Current review of IDA's portfolio in Djibouti indicates that audit reports are not received on time. To help
ensure that audit reports for this project are received on time, the appointment of an independent extemal
auditor with qualifications and experience acceptable to IDA under agreed terms of reference is a Condition
of Effectiveness. Audit reports must be submitted to IDA within three months following the fiscal year-end
of the project implementing organization. The Credit Agreement requires that these auditing arrangements
remain in place for the duration of the project.
- 18 -



3. Technical:
The Djibouti Roads Department and Ethiopian Roads Authority agreed in early 1999 that the roadway had
deteriorated so significantly that it would be easier to drive on if the surface layer were removed. This was
implemented jointly in the spring of 1999, and since mid-1999, trucks have been riding directly on the base
course of the existing road between Dikhil and Galafi. This has caused the base course material to be
crushed by heavy trucks and, presently there is deep rutting and large sections with corrugations
developing, making the ride extremely rough. This has destroyed the physical properties of the existing
layers, making rehabilitation more complicated and costly. There are also signs that oversized material has
been used for the base course and the sub-base layers in some places, indicating inadequate quality control.
The only option seems to be to reconstruct the pavement on the Dikhil to Galafi section (102 km) and on a
15 km long section of Arta turnoff- Dikhil in the Petit Bara area. This would be carried out by installing a
new crushed base course after grading and compacting the existing running surface, followed by a
regulating sub-base course layer. The plan is to put on a black top that can last about 5 years (single or
double surface dressing). The design standard would be a 6.5 m carriageway with no shoulders. If the EU
project follows the same alignment, it would then widen the road to provide full shoulders and asphalt
concrete surfacing, except that in some sections the EU financed project is expected to provide bypass
roads at major settlements along the road. Repair and other maintenance work will also have to be done on
some culverts and many of the "Irish crossings", some of which have collapsed completely, and these would
need to be completely rebuilt under the EU-financed project.
There is no shortage of material along the road for crushed base course. There are several existing material
sites mostly in wadis, from the time when the road was first constructed, and these can be opened again and
used for the the construction works. Water for compaction is necessary and generally available, though it
may have to be transported over some distance for locations where the water table is very low. To address
these implementation details and facilitate a speedy implementation of the project, three consultants
financed under the PHRD Grant will arrive in Djibouti in May to: (i) review the existing material sites and
to verify which ones to use, (ii) deternine which bore-holes to use for supply of construction water, (iii)
prepare concept pavement designs to verify that the original design of the road is the best way of
restoration/reconstruction, (iv) prepare plans for force account work, and (v) prepare typical standardized
bidding documents for material production and sub-contracting ahead of the arrival of the Construction
Manager. These consultants have been identified and are expected to have largely completed their
assignment by end-June.
These plans would be coordinated with the Ethiopian Roads Authority to ensure continued maintenance of
the road beyond the border.
4. Institutional:
4.1 Executing agencies:
The Project Implementation Unit (PIU) of the Ministry of Equipment and Transport would be the
Government's Executing Agency for this operation. It would be strengthened to take on this task by the
recruitment of a Construction Management team and by the appointment of a Resident Supervision
Engineer.
- 19 -



4.2 Project management:
The most appropriate contracting arrangement has been considered carefully, due to the urgent nature of
the works, the inadequate implementing capacity of the Roads Department, and a weak local road
construction industry. The appointment of a construction manager has been accepted in principle by the
Government as the best way to manage the project. The manager would make the final decision on the
most efficient use of National Competitive Bidding (NCB) with local and regional contractors for works
and aggregates production, as well as the use of the force account unit for some works and aggregates
production. The present plan of the Government is to have three smaller contracts, each about 20-25 km
long, carried out by local contractors, and a section of about 30 km undertaken by one or two force account
units. The force account unit would also be used for maintenance works and early grading of the road.
4.3 Procurement issues:
There is a need to act quickly, and shorten the bidding and contract award period. In the past, award of
contracts in the transport sector has taken a long time, as much as 7 months between completion of the
evaluation report and final contract signature. This excessive time to award contracts needs to be avoided
for the proposed project, and the proposed implementation arrangements have been designed to reduce the
approval process to one month, and the number of signatures by Government officials from as many as 7
signatures to 2 only. The procurement risk rating is 'modest'. Normally, the Roads Department of the
MOET would have been the implementing agency. However, due to the weak capacity of the department
and the difficulty involved in strengthening it to be responsible for this project when salaries of staff cannot
be guaranteed, the option of setting up an independent Project Implementation Unit (PIU) within the
Ministry of Equipment and Transport has been chosen. To reduce the significant procurement risks the
project has been strengthened with a Construction Manager to assist in undertaking all bidding, evaluation
and award of contracts under the project. The PIU would have to review and endorse the CM's
recommendations and provide two signatories on any contract, one by the PIU Director and the other by the
Minister of Finance or a person from his ministry delegated this responsibility. The possibility of force
account operations would also reduce the procurement risks under the Project. Works would be bid on the
basis of documents prepared by international specialists employed by the Roads Department of MOET
using available Japanese grant (PHRD) funds. The bidding documents would be ready by Board, and will
be issued shortly after the Construction Manager has had time to review the drafts and take ownership of
the design and bidding documents. These actions should enable contract work activity in the field well
before the end of the year.
4.4 Financial management issues:
The main risks facing the Project are the uncertainty of proper pricing of the contracts and the possible
diversion of funds during implementation for purposes other than for the Project. These risks have been
mitigated through the appointment of an international Construction Manager to manage the contracts and
the force account work force. The CM would be responsible for the setting up of accounting arrangements
for the force account unit, and these arrangements would be designed to be sustainable even after project
completion. The appointment of a Supervising Engineer with broad international quality control
experience, and the setting up of a separate PIU equipped with qualified, adequately remunerated staff
would supplement this effort and secure good financial management. The appointment of the accountant for
the PIU is a Condition of Effectiveness.
5. Environmental:                  Environmental Category: B (Partial Assessment)
5.1 Summarize the steps undertaken for environmental assessment and EMP preparation (including
consultation and disclosure) and the significant issues and their treatment emerging from this analysis.
- 20 -



No major environmental issues are anticipated for this project, since it represents rehabilitation of an
existing road only, with no changes in the alignment. There are some minor impacts associated with repair
and upgrading of the road, including: removal and disposal of waste materials from road bed and disposal
of containers of bitumen and other types of sealers; risk of localized pollution of land and soils from
disposal of tires, broken parts, waste fuel, and used lubricants for heavy machinery and trucks engaged in
construction; temporary aggravation of noise and dust levels during construction; potential damage to local
vegetation by provision of temporary bypass roads during construction. Quarrying and materials gathering
activities could locally increase soil erosion from wind and water on a temporary basis. Some limited
changes may occur in runoff patterns in seasonal watercourses from these activities. Induced demand is
often a potential environmental concern on road projects. However, given the short-term, temporary nature
of this project, it is not anticipated that induced demand would be significant from an economic or
enviromnental perspective, since the road has historically had very little passenger travel, and the freight
traffic between Djibouti and Addis (predominantly petroleum products, fertilizer, and food aid) is rather
inelastic to travel time or cost in the short-run.
5.2 What are the main features of the EMP and are they adequate?
An Environmental Management Plan (EMP) has been prepared for the project in coordination with the
Ministry of Habitat, Urban Development, Environment and Territorial Planning (MOE). It includes
specific measures to mitigate potential construction and operation period impacts and to address road
safety issues. Key activities in the EMP include: contract requirements for proper management of
construction waste; control measures for waste fuel, oil and lubricants; reduction of noise and dust levels;
and rehabilitation of areas used for bypass roads and construction materials. Construction contracts will
include specific environmental provisions and contractors will receive a briefing concerning these
requirements during the site visit. The plan includes measures for improvement and monitoring of road
safety. Provisions have been included for the use of "chance find procedures" for buried or unrecorded
archaeological and historical sites, including agreement with the Institut Superieur d'Etudes et de
Recherches Scientifiques et Techniques (ISERST) to have an archaeologist on-site during important earth
work and materials gathering activities. The archaeologist would be attached to the Construction Manager's
team and provided transport and office space at the road camp in Dikhil. A list of known sites in the region
has been compiled, and will be provided to the Construction Manager, Supervising Engineer, and individual
contractors and discussed during the environmental briefing.
5.3 For Category A and B projects, timeline and status of EA:
Date of receipt of final draft: May 8, 2000
The environmental category is justified, since the project represents rehabilitation of an existing road only,
with no changes in the alignment. The environmental impacts associated with the proposed road
rehabilitation activities would be limited in scope and duration. The potential impacts of project supported
activities are well known and can be effectively addressed through engineering design, low cost mitigation
measures and proper construction monitoring. These mitigation and monitoring activities would be a
responsibility of the Construction Manager (see TOR in Annex 13).
5.4 How have stakeholders been consulted at the stage of (a) environmental screening and (b) draft EA
report on the environmental impacts and proposed environment management plan? Describe mechanisms
of consultation that were used and which groups were consulted?
Consultations with stakeholders from national and local government, embassies and international
organizations, applied research institutes, private sector and nongovernmental organizations have been
conducted to support project identification and preparation. Public consultation meetings have been held in
Djibouti City and in the project area in cooperation with the implementing agency and MOE. A draft EMP
- 21 -



was circulated at these meetings and their minutes are included in the EMP. In addition to representatives
of national and local government, these meetings involved representatives from local nongovernmental
organizations (Association ALBIB, Association al Biri, Association of Women for Development - Dikhil
and Chamber of Commerce); embassies and international organizations (EC, France, IMF, UNDP, United
States and World Food Program) and village elders. The meetings were also covered in the national press
and on radio. The Borrower has made the EMP (see Annex 12) available in English and French to the
public at the MOE, the Offices of the Dikhil and Djibouti Districts and the InfoShop of the World Bank.
5.5 What mechanisms have been established to monitor and evaluate the impact of the project on the
environment? Do the indicators reflect the objectives and results of the EMP?
The Construction Manager would include specific actions to monitor the impact of the project on the
environment during the construction phase, and a program for monitoring of road accidents during the
operational stage would be developed during the Phase I works and agreed at the Early Implementation
Assessment. Specific provisions concerning environmental, health and safety issues will be included in
construction contracts, and the Construction Manager would follow up on this. A series of key indicators
have been selected for monitoring implementation of the EMP: proper collection and disposal of
construction and equipment related wastes; routine regrading of material collection sites after use; and
regular use of archaeological support and application of archaeological "chance find" procedures. At the
operational level improvements in road safety would also be monitored in the future according to the plan
developed under the project.
6. Social:
6.1 Summarize key social issues relevant to the project objectives, and specify the projects social
development outcomes.
The project preparation process has identified three social issues: (a) access to construction period
employment by local residents in the project area; (b) potential for increased transmission of HIV/AIDS at
construction sites and along the rehabilitated transport corridor; and (c) a need to improve road safety to
reduce the high level of accidents.
(a) Local Employment. The primary social issue from a community perspective is the access to unskilled
jobs associated with construction activities related to the rehabilitation of the road. It was widely
recognized during the Public Consultation process that access to road-rehabilitation-related unskilled
employment in the project area is to be organized by the local population, in accordance with custom
established by village elders and in collaboration with the Commissar of the District of Dikhil. Local
leaders in the project area have pledged to assist in seeing that this process goes smoothly. Since the
primary objective of the proposed credit is to secure unhampered and continued road access on Djibouti
Port's transit corridor for the next five years, the creation of employment, while a beneficial side-effect of
the project, is not considered to be an expected social development outcome.
(b) HIV/AIDS. Risk of HIV/AIDS infection is a significant problem in Djibouti and Ethiopia, and
construction sites and the rehabilitated corridor itself may become an important conduit of HIV/AIDS, as
roadways and trucking operations have proven to be elsewhere in Africa. Currently, Djibouti is ranked 12th
and Ethiopia 16th in terms of prevalence of HIV/AIDS in Africa, with an HIV/AIDS infection rate of 10.3
and 9.3 percent respectively. Evidence suggests that construction sites and road transport facilities can be
important conduits of HIV/AIDS, potentially exacerbated by the behavior of construction workers and
truck drivers who tend to be away from their families for extended periods of time. While the project cannot
purport to address such a difficult health problem, technical assistance activities undertaken early before
implementation of the project would facilitate the design and implementation of an appropriate HI4V/AIDS
related intervention for persons living along the road corridor, for the trucking industry and for construction
- 22 -



crews. The World Health Organization is administering funds for a program on HIV/AIDS, implemented
by the Ministry of Health. One component of this program is intended to target specifically economic and
social activities associated with RN 1. The technical assistance to be initiated before Project
implementation will examine mechanisms of cooperation in such an effort.
(c) Road Safety. Current poor physical conditions, high dust levels, poor vehicle standards and routine
overloading contribute to frequent road accidents with associated injuries and fatalities. The Project will
support implementation of measures for axle-load control and routine inspections to reduce overloading and
excessive dimensions. Technical assistance for advisory and planning services and training for a road
safety study and priority implementation measures will also be undertaken. This will include evaluation of
the incidence and causes of accidents, identification of measures that can be taken to reduce accidents and
priority actions to be implemented under the Project. The preparation of this study will be coordinated with
the Port of Djibouti since outbound trucks originate in the port.
6.2 Participatory Approach: How are key stakeholders participating in the project?
During project identification, Bank staff met with representatives of Dikhil District, the principal local
jurisdiction through which the road passes. Their views have been incorporated into the design and
justification of the project, and their advice has continued to be sought during project preparation. Formal
consultations with local communities, including village elders, were held in Dikhil, and these suggested
strong support for the project.
6.3 How does the project involve consultations or collaboration with NGOs or other civil society
organizations?
As noted in Section 5.4 a range of stakeholders has been consulted in identification and preparation of the
project and this process is planned to continue during the implementation phase. This process has involved
private sector interests associated with the Port of Djibouti and the international and local trucking
industry. Implementation of the project would include regular consultations with representatives of the
Ethiopian and Djiboutian trucking industries and meetings with local government, village leaders, elders
and nongovernmental organizations in the Dikhil and Djibouti Districts. Given the short-term focus of the
project on physical works for road rehabilitation, it is anticipated that the collaboration with NGOs or other
civil society organizations would be focused on the HIV/AIDS education and outreach program which
provides an opportunity to work with groups concerned with rural health, community development and
women.
6.4 What institutional arrangements have been provided to ensure the project achieves its social
development outcomes?
The PIU will coordinate activities regarding the development of an appropriately targeted HIV/AIDS
education and outreach program with the Ministry of Health and the World Health Organization in
Djibouti. In addition, the PIU will assist in coordination of the TA program that will follow up on
identification and development of potential employment opportunities in trade-related sectors.
6.5 How will the project monitor performance in terms of social development outcomes?
The specific mechanisms would be discussed and agreed at the Early Implementation Assessment for the
Project.
