7.2 Describe provisions made by the project to ensure compliance with applicable safeguard policies.
An Environmental Management Plan has been prepared that addresses the limited environmental issues
associated with the road rehabilitation activities proposed for funding. Provisions have been made in the
project design and budget to support implementation of the EMP. As a preventive measure ISERST will
provide an archaeologist to conduct on-site monitoring of major earthworks and materials collection and
use of archaeological chance find procedures.
F. Sustainability and Risks
1. Sustainability:
Economic sustainability. The project is intended as an interim solution to the rapidly deteriorating
condition of the road in the Djibouti portion of the Djibouti to Addis-Ababa road. The longer, more
sustainable connection between these two nodes is currently under study with assistance from the EC
Delegation in Ethiopia. The principal economic risk associated with the proposed International Road
Corridor Rehabilitation Credit, then, concerns the nature and timing of the future EU-financed project. Any
quantifiable assessment of that risk needs to be weighed against the fact that the proposed credit affords the
proposed EU-financed project more time to make a considered judgement on the best option and design for
that longer term road, a benefit which is by its nature not quantifiable. The risk that traffic would shift back
to Assab during the short project period is negligible. Even if there were a sudden peace in the very near
future, it is difficult to imagine a resumption of traffic at Assab at the same levels as before the war for the
foreseeable future. In fact, a resolution of the crisis increases the likelihood of more traffic along the
Galafi-Djibouti road, because of Ethiopia's transition from a war economy increasing both imports and
exports, heightening the need for the proposed project.
The pre-feasibility study of the EU-assisted road project will examine at least two alignments for the
Djibouti - Addis road: the existing alignment, and a new road through Dire Dawa. If the existing alignment
is eventually chosen, the sustainability and risk associated with the proposed IDA credit relate to the timing
of such a project. The longer the preparation time of the EU-financed project, the higher the economic
return on the present project, although at a certain point, the risk of rapid road deterioration and high
maintenance costs will again begin to outweigh that return. It is difficult to predict when such deterioration
might begin to occur, however. The prudent course of action, then, is to design the road assuming the
proposed EU-financed project would be completed in five years, following start of construction in 2002. In
the event of a delay in the EU-financed project, revenues from the RMF should be able to keep the road in
reasonable condition for a period of time. A maintenance contract for the first three years is therefore
included as a locally financed component of the project (RMF). This contract will be designed to allow for
annual extensions beyond the initial three-year term. Effort has been made to design a project that could be
fully incorporated into any EU proposal to use the same alignment; consequently, the salvage value of the
- 24 -



proposed IDA-financed project will be quite high if the same alignment is followed. If the Dire Dawa
alignment is chosen, then the economic value of the proposed Djibouti-Galafi rehabilitation credit following
completion of the EU-assisted project would be predominantly in the form of choice value of routes to
Addis. At that point, maintenance requirements on the Dikhil-Galafi section of the road would reduce
significantly, since most of the traffic would divert to the Dire Dawa road (assuming no distortionary
incentives are in place).
Institutional sustainability. In view of the emergency nature and urgency of this operation, resolution of
sectoral and institutional issues in the middle of efforts to respond to the emergency could be
counterproductive. For this reason the project has been given a two-phased design, where the first phase is
entirely directed at addressing the urgency of restoring normal transport operation on the road corridor.
The important long-term issue of institutional sustainability will be pursued during the implementation of
the second phase of the Project. It should be noted that the Government is strongly committed to
institutional reform/development along the lines of the Africa Road Management Initiative, and hence the
risk of Phase II not being implemented is relatively small. During the first year, a detailed analysis would
be undertaken of the institutional re-arrangement options and how the financing of the road sector could be
improved. It is also expected that the Construction Manager would assist in showing the effectiveness of a
more commercialized management of road sector operations, and in the gradual phasing in of private sector
provision of road maintenance and construction services. However, given the small size of this country, the
relatively small size of the road network, the frequent collapse of drainage structures during the rainy
season and the apparent lack of a well functioning market for road services, there is a case for keeping a
force account capability to provide some road services. Training for the Force Account Unit, and the
responsibility for its operations would also be part of the TOR for the Construction Manager. The future
role of Force Account operations in the road sector would be considered during the institutional studies.
Sectoral sustainability. The sustainability of the road sector depends on timely maintenance and adherence
to the axle load limits. The off-budget financing of maintenance through the newly established RMF would
help to remove funding constraints for the project roads. A new government policy of adopting private
sector provision of routine and periodic maintenance should help in overcoming institutional constraints
through contracting out of maintenance services directly by the RMF. The EU financed study is expected
to address this and provide guidelines. Assistance under the project to support the construction of axle load
control stations and related equipment would help to reduce truck overloading and unnecessary damages to
the roads.
Financial sustainability. The availability of fiscal resources to support this Project should not be a
problem. The Road Maintenance Fund is expected to generate about US$ 1.4 million per year, which
should be adequate for the maintenance of the transit route itself; in the future, however, as traffic
increases, the revenues may not be sufficient to allow for the maintenance of the road network. It should be
noted that the recently negotiated truck tariff of $0.50/ton is not consistent with either long-term needs or
regional COMESA arrangements. During the Early Implementation Assessment, this tariff would be
reviewed and a recommendation made on a more appropriate tariff.

Country Risk: Political instability in the region is a potential risk to the project. The major rehabilitation
works planned are adjacent to the border with Ethiopia in an area that has in the past been subject to
fighting between Eritrean and Ethiopian troops. While the likelihood of this affecting areas inside Djibouti
are small, the operation of the Djibouti - Addis Ababa Corridor is subject to these uncertainties, and
therefore, such hostilities should be considered a risk of the project.

None.
G. Main Credit Conditions
1. Effectiveness Condition
As conditions of effectiveness, the Government should:
(a) Appointment of the Construction Manager and the Supervision Engineer with terms of reference
acceptable to IDA;
(b) Appointment of a qualified accountant with terms of reference acceptable to IDA;
(c) The establishment of a project financial management system, including a manual accounting system,
internal control mechanisms and documented policies and procedures; and
(d) Appointment of a private auditor with terms of referenceacceptable to IDA.
2. Other [classify according to covenant types used in the Legal Agreements.]
(a) As condition of Board Presentation, GD has provided IDA with a signed Letter of Road Sector Policy.
(b) As a condition of Board presentation, GD has established the Project Implementation Unit and
appointed the Project Director.
- 27 -



(c) As a credit covenant, GD will maintain the agreed external audit arrangements and will submit audited
financial statements not later than three months after the end of each fiscal year.
(d) Within one year of credit effectiveness GD will conduct an Early Implementation Assessment and
agree with IDA on a program of institutional development to be implemented under Phase II of the
project.

Annex 2: Project Description
DJIBOUTI: INTERNATIONAL ROAD CORRIDOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Detailed Project Description
Background
Djibouti currently serves as the primary transit port for landlocked Ethiopia following the outbreak of
armed conflict between Ethiopia and Eritrea in 1998. Assab, on the Eritrean coast, formerly played this
role, but with the sudden shift in 1998 of traffic from Assab Port to Djibouti Port, the 20 year old road
connection between Djibouti Port and the Addis-Assab Road (via Dikhil and Galafi) was suddenly required
to carry a level of heavy-vehicle traffic it was neither designed for nor in a condition to carry.
The lack of regular maintenance within Djibouti has contributed to the accelerated deterioration of this 188
km road section, which originally had a double surface dressing over a natural gravel base/sub-base.
Portions of the road are presently disintegrating, posing a risk of complete failure on long sections. On the
102 km section closest to the border, all bituminous surfacing has either been destroyed by traffic or
removed to improve rideability, and the road is kept passable by occasional grading. On the 86 km road
section closest to Djibouti city (Arta turnoff to Dikhil) about 18 km has recently been repaired by a local
contractor. However, this work is very short term and already requires maintenance repairs. The rest,
partly in a state of rapid deterioration, is causing accidents and is slowing down truck traffic significantly.
Maintaining this 188 km road link is critical to Djibouti Port's ability to serve the Ethiopian traffic, and
remain competitive in the long-run. The section of the route within Ethiopia is in good condition or is being
improved under another IDA credit approved in FY98 (Ethiopia - Road Sector Development Program
Support Credit - Cr. 3032-ET). The Djibouti Government recently requested the World Bank to assist in
financing limited, but adequate, rehabilitation of the road pavement as well as emergency repairs to
drainage structures, and to ensure that the 188 km road section will be well maintained thereafter.
Objective
The objective of the project is to secure, over the medium-term, an acceptable level of service on the
Djibouti-Galafi Road which constitutes Djibouti Port's transit corridor to Ethiopia, during the period when
a long-term corridor improvement scheme is being developed and implemented by the Ethiopian and
Djibouti governments with assistance from the European Union.
By Component:
Project Component I - US$12.00 million
Civil Works between Dikhil and Galafi: The works involve full reconstruction of the pavement on the 102
km Dikhil to Galafi section of the National Road No. 1. This would be carried out by installing a new
crushed base course of 15 cm thickness. The existing severely deteriorated road way would be graded and
compacted, followed by the placement of a 15 cm thick regulating sub-base layer of screened natural gravel
compacted. The works include a black top of double bituminous surface dressing. This type of structure is
expected to last at least 5 years without resurfacing. Repair and other maintenance work will also have to
be done on many of the Irish crossings and culverts, some of which have collapsed completely. The road
width (surface dressing) would be limited to 6.5 meters on a 7 meter base course. If the EU financed
project follow the same alignment, it would widen the road with a new lane for the Ethiopian bound heavy
trucks and a full width shoulder, while the 6.5 meter project road would serve as a travel lane for Djibouti
bound (light truck) traffic and provide a full width surface dressed shoulder of 3 meters. The EU project is
also expected to provide an asphalt concrete surfacing of adequate thickness thereby providing an expected
necessary strengthening of the project road after 5 years service.
- 32 -



The civil works are expected to be implemented using local contractors undertaking three contracts of
about 20-25 km each. Civil works to complete the remaining 30 km are expected to be carried out by a
force account unit(s).
Project Component 2 - US$2.00 million
Civil Works between Arta and Dikhil: The works involve full reconstruction of the pavement on a 15 km
long section of Arta turnoff- Dikhil in the Petit Bara area. The works and standards would be the same as
for the 102 km Dikhil - Galafi road section (see above). In addition, the works would include pothole
repairs on other sections of this 86 km road length and limited resurfacing to preserve the road surface from
further deterioration and extend the life by another couple of years until the EU financed project can
provide a more permanent restoration and improvement. The 15 km road contract would be tendered
locally or carried out by force account.
Project Component 3 - US$ 0.50 million
Rehabilitate Roads Department's Eguipment Pool: An international equipment specialist has inspected the
fleet of equipment and determined rehabilitation needs. The best pieces for use in the force account
operations on the project will be selected by the Construction Manager. An updated list of repairs and
parts (with cost estimates) required to bring all the usable equipment up to a level of good operating
condition, is being prepared by the same specialist.
Project Component 4 - US$0.50 million
Routine Maintenance of the entire Arta-spur to Galafi: A performance based road maintenance contract
for 3 years would be awarded to a local contractor to preserve the full 188 km road section and in
particular the Irish crossings and other drainage structures to provide unrestricted flow of traffic also
during rains. This maintenance concession would be extendable and would be based on the outcome of the
EU financed study on road maintenance by contracts, and assist in providing experience with this form of
regular road maintenance. This component would be funded by the Road Maintenance Fund.
Project Component 5 - US$0.35 million
Axle Load Control station: The project would finance the construction of two small control stations, one
near Djibouti City and another at the border post of Galafi. These two stations would control vehicle
dimensions, axle loads and gross vehicle weights and carry out road worthiness checks of vehicles entering
Djibouti to make sure that vehicle lights etc. are in good working order. The cost of manning these stations
would be the responsibility of the Road Maintenance Fund.
Equipment for the Weigh Bridge Station: The project would finance the necessary axle load control scales
and the recording instruments, accurate enough to allow enforcement of axle load limits and gross vehicle
weight limits. The equipment is expected to be located close to the port and at Galafi, and will be operated
by the RMF staff collecting road user charges.
Project Component 6 - US$0.25 million
Project Implementation Unit: The project would be implemented by a Project Implementation Unit (PIU)
established within the Ministry of Equipment and Transport (MOET). This unit would be based in
Djibouti city and delegated full authority by the Government to make efficient managerial decisions and to
authorize all payments under the Project. The PIU would operate the Financial Management System and
the Special Account for the project. The Project Director, in charge of the PIU, should have sufficient
rank to obtain quick decisions from the country's top decision makers and should have the capacity to
execute decisions rapidly. The Government would provide office space for the unit and second staff to it.
- 33 -



However, other costs related to the unit would be financed under the project. These costs are related to the
establishment of a financial management system, accounting and auditing, and the processing of invoices
and claims, and would include the cost of staff recruited from outside civil service.
Project Component 7 - US$1.30 million
Construction management consultant and supervisor of the works consultant:
Construction Manager (CM). The objectives of the assignment are to provide construction management
services through the pre-construction, construction and start of the maintenance phase. The construction
manager's main goal is to ensure that the work is properly completed in accordance with the project
specification and schedule.
The manager will select the contracting methodology, including, but not limited to, competitively bid
contracts for works and goods, and use of the MOET equipment and personnel to perform works, and
maintenance contracts. The manager will use previously prepared bidding documents, perform bid
evaluations and award construction contracts with the concurrence of the PIU. Supervision of the works
will be by other consultants who will report directly to PIU. The manager will also select and manage
MOET equipment, personnel and a road camp and repair shop at Dikhil. This will in effect create a force
account unit to allow the CM an additional option for carrying out the repair and maintenance of the road.
A preliminary assessment is that the team of the CM would comprise at least, a Project Manager (with
experience of construction, repair and maintenance of national roads), a general field superintendent and a
supervisor for the force account unit. The equipment operators, mechanics, laborers, office staff, force
account staff, and some field survey staff will be provided by the Government, and their availability has
been confirmed.
The construction manager will manage the works and administer the works contracts and ensure that
contractual clauses whether related to quality or quantities of work, are carried out. It will determine the
most effective and economical way to implement the project. In carrying out his duties, the construction
manager will ensure that the provisions of the Environmental Management Plan are implemented. He will
also be responsible for safety and efficiently rerouting traffic during construction, and will have to secure
that the movement of truck traffic during construction is no worse than before construction.
The Supervision Engineer would provide pre-construction and construction supervision services. The
scope of the services will include:
(a)    verification, adjustment where necessary, and assumption of responsibility for the following items
prepared previously by specialist consultants:
(i)    an outline pavement design(s) to bituminous standard and typical cross-sections for the
rehabilitation of the road to carry the projected traffic for a relatively short life span of 5
years;
(ii)   the scope of temporary repairs of minor drainage structures along the road consisting of
concrete box culverts and Irish crossings ; and
(b)    supervising the works contracts on behalf of MOET with regard to quality assurance and
contractual compliance including the following activities:
(i)    supervising the construction works contracts to ensure that contractual clauses, whether
related to quality or quantities of work, are respected;
(ii)    undertaking the necessary quality control of works;
(iii)   making all engineering decisions - including improvement of designs, if necessary -
required for the good implementation of the contract;
- 34 -



(iv)   making amendments to or extensions of the sources of materials identified previously by
the specialist consultants to meet the necessary production targets.
The supervision team would include at least a Chief Supervision Engineer and one Supervision Engineer
with extensive experience of road construction supervision and a sound knowledge of materials testing and
utilization in pavements as well as thorough familiarity of quality control procedures in environments
similar to that prevailing in the project area. A number of government support staff would be required to
support the supervision engineer (surveyors, soils technicians, inspectors, etc).
Project Component 8 - US$0.25 million
Offices, Vehicles and labaratory Equipment for Supervising Engineer, Construction Manager and PIU:
This is normally provided by the construction contractor as part of the contract. In order to avoid
duplication and possibly make a saving on the provision of these facilities, the procurement of these
facilities would be carried out by the Construction Manager through international shopping procedures.
The facilities will be managed by the force account unit during implementation of the works, and would be
taken over by the new road administration at the end of the construction works.
Project Component 9 - US$0.50 million
Institutional Development Support: A framework agreement between the government and IDA on the
future direction of the road sector, including management and financing of roads was agreed as part of the
preparation of the project. This sub-component of the project would finance agreed studies, workshops and
other necessary actions during the first 12 months of the Project to facilitate a process of defining the
institutional transformation necessary as well as actions to achieve financial sustainability of the road
sector with concrete steps and a time bound program. This program would be discussed and agreed with
the government at an Early Implementation Assessment of the Project, and a three-year action program
would introduce effective institutional arrangements and full off budget financing of the road sector. The
sub-component would support this transformation. Terms of Reference for this sub-component was
discussed and agreed at negotiations and is included as Annex 16.
Project Component 10 - US$0.25 million
Studies on HIV/AIDS and Road Safety: Risk of HIV/AIDS infection is a significant problem in Djibouti
and Ethiopia, and the rehabilitated road corridor may become an important conduit of HIV/AIDS, as
roadways and trucking operations have proven to be elsewhere in Africa. Currently, Djibouti is ranked 12th
and Ethiopia 16th in terms of prevalence of HIV/AIDS in Africa, with an HIV/AIDS infection rate of 10.3
and 9.3 percent respectively. While the project cannot purport to address such a difficult health problem,
this sub-component would undertake technical assistance activities early before completion of the project to
facilitate the design and implementation of appropriate HIV/AIDS related interventions targeting
specifically economic and social activities associated with RN 1, the trucking industry and construction and
maintenance crews. A small traffic safety study (road safety audits will also be carried out in order to
define measures to be undertaken to reduce truck accidents.
Project Component 11 - US$0.10 million
Study on Development of Trade-related Services in Diibouti: The long-term development of a
Djibouti-Addis corridor is an important element in the development strategy for the Djiboutian economy. It
would provide Djiboutians with a sustainable development path focusing on a wide range of employment
opportunities from port and rail services, trucking and freight forwarding, to banking, hotel and
entertainment services. This small TA sub-component is included to assist the Djibouti Government
formulate a strategy for development of these trade-related services in Djibouti. The study would define a
corridor development program requiring a clearly specified and phased set of transport development
- 35 -



actions. These should encompass both physical infrastructure and institutional development spanning the
port, road, trucking and rail sectors. The program must take into account the transportation planning of
other affected countries in the region, and aim at harmonizing important aspects of transport policy, such
as regulations (e.g. axle load limits, through bills of lading, etc.). TORs for this assistance are included in
Annex 13.

Cost Estimate of the Civil Works
In order ro refine the cost estimate for the civil works, a cost estimate for a thirty five (35) km section of
Dikhil - Galafi road repair work was made based on observations of the condition of the road by the
appraisal mission in April, 2000. The mission estimated the cost based on repairing the road to a standard
that will sustain over three to four hundred heavily loaded tandem trucks travelling on the road in each
direction over a five-year period. The repair method selected consists of scarifying and preparing the badly
deteriorated sub base to receive an average of 15 cm of gravel to restore the sub grade. 15 cm of a crushed
stone base will next be placed followed by a bitumen double surface treatment. Repair of concrete drifts
and Irish crossings and cleaning of culverts is included as well as the costs of detours for traffic off the
existing road. The costs so determined have been extrapolated to estimate the full 102 km of highway from
Dikhil to Galafi if implemented in two or three contracts and one 30 km force account section. The section
to be executed by force account is assumed to be as expensive as the sections implemented by private
contractors. Also the repair works at the Petit Bara section is expected to be executed in the same manner
and estimated to cost as much per kilometer as the Dikhil-Galafi section. The table below provides unit
prices used to develop the estimate for the 35 km section, which was then extrapolated to arrive at the total
cost of civil works. Detailed cost estimate based on labor costs, equipment usage, productivity, etc. is
available in the Project File.

Annex 4: Cost Benefit Analysis Summary
DJIBOUTI: INTERNATIONAL ROAD CORRIDOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Introduction
The International Development Association is considering a credit to the Republic of Djibouti of about
US$15.0 million for the urgent rehabilitation of the portions of National Road 1, going from Djibouti Port
to the settlement of Galafi at the Ethiopian border. The road has served as the primary communication
corridor for goods arriving by sea destined for Addis Ababa and the rest of Ethiopia since the outbreak of
armed conflict between Ethiopia and Eritrea in 1998. Since then, its condition (already weakened by
movements of heavy military equipment in the previous war between the countries) has deteriorated
significantly. Today, the portion of the road between Dikhil and Galafi has lost its bituminous surfacing,
with some sections barely passable. The section between the Arta Spur Road and Dikhil has received some
maintenance attention through a French grant, but even this aid has not been sufficient to stave off
deterioration. Only the section of the roadway between the Arta Spur Road and the entrance to Djibouti
City is in good condition, owing to a high quality reconstruction financed by the Italian government.
The European Union is currently financing a regional study of options for the longer term development of
the Djibouti-Addis Ababa corridor. Options under consideration include reconstruction of the existing
alignment, re-alignment and new construction along the same route, and development of a new route,
through Ali Sabieh and Dire Dawa. Significant road rehabilitation and upgrading works are already
underway in Ethiopia for the section of road between Addis Ababa and Dire Dawa, through a World Bank
credit. In addition, the Agence Francaise de Developpement (AFD) is currently looking at options to
develop and upgrade the existing rail corridor between Djibouti and Addis Ababa, which follows the Dire
Dawa route.
Whatever option the study identifies, however, it is unlikely that it could be designed and implemented in
less than five years. In the meantime, the Dikhil to Galafi section of National Road 1 is likely to deteriorate
substantially particularly following the rainy season and it is questionable whether the road can remain
passable during that period. The proposed IDA credit, therefore, is intended to an acceptable level of
service in the short the (next five years) while the longer term corridor development project is designed and
implemented. However, both programs are being planned so that, in the event the current alignment is
chosen for the EU-supported project, the rehabilitated road can be integrated into the longer-term road
solution, taking advantage of the investments already made.
Summary of Benefits and Costs:
Benefits of the proposed credit: The Port of Djibouti and the transport of goods from the port to
neighboring countries are the primary engines of the Djiboutian economy. Its strategic location on the Red
Sea's international shipping routes, as well as in relation to its East African nations, potentially make
Djibouti an excellent entrepot for the region. For various reasons related to geo-politics and infrastructure
deficiencies, this potential has barely begun to be tapped. With unemployment rates reportedly as high as
40 percent, development of the trade and transport sectors, which could generate considerable employment
in the local economy, is clearly a high priority for the Government.
Since 1998, Djibouti Port has effectively been a monopoly supplier of port services to Ethiopia. This
situation, however, is an accidental result of regional geo-politics; once the geo-political situation changes,
the privileged monopoly position of Djibouti Port may disappear. Consequently, Djibouti needs to act
quickly to consolidate its competitive position.
- 39 -



Several aspects of port operation are key to bolstering its competitiveness against other Ports in the region
(Assab, Port Sudan, and Berbera) which may challenge Djibouti for the Ethiopian transit market. Efficient
port operations, competitive rate structures, and the reliability of the entire logistics chain, from dockside to
Addis-Ababa are among these. The poor condition of the road severely hampers the reliability of this
logistics chain, and constitutes a significant deficiency which could hinder Djibouti's ability to compete.
Improving service performance of the roadway is of significant benefit to Djibouti, providing short-term
improvements to the reliability of the Port connection, and enhancing the long term image and
competitiveness of the Port.
Quantitatively, this benefit to Djibouti is at least as great as the reduction in vehicle operating costs along
the roadway, since this is the reduction in costs which shippers would need to receive to make them at least
economically indifferent to using another equidistant port to which road conditions are reasonably good, eg
the port of Assab. (Prior to the war, the road to Assab was a high-quality, smooth-surface, two-lane road.
It is not clear what effect the war has had on the quality of the road.) So while reductions in vehicle
operating costs and travel times immediately benefit Ethiopian truckers, they ultimately accrue back to
Djibouti in strengthened competitiveness. For this reason, truck VOC reduction is used as a proxy for
strengthened competitiveness of Djibouti Port in this analysis.
Another benefit related to the above considerations is the reduced likelihood of a breakdown of the corridor
during the period that a longer term EU-financed transport link is under development. As indicated earlier,
the road, particularly between Dikhil and Galafi, is in a very poor condition. The bituminous surfacing had
to be removed, and the other pavement layers (sub-base and base courses) are disintegrating, under the
weight of trucks operating directly on the base course. Some of the Irish crossings have crumbled, and in
these areas, movements of earth for example, following heavy rains might make the road impassable. It is
difficult to know what the magnitude of such a breakdown might be, or how long it would take to re-open
the road. Consequently, it is hard to predict how such an event would affect logistics managers decisions,
in terms of sending goods via Djibouti, and therefore hard to understand the short and long term impacts on
the Djibouti economy. Such an event, however, would no doubt negatively impact Djibouti's image, both
among importers and within the Ethiopian govenmnent. Reducing the likelihood of such an event, therefore,
constitutes a significant, if unquantifiable, benefit.
Several other benefits are also unquantifiable, but not negligible. First is the need to develop the long-term
solution for the Djibouti to Addis Ababa corridor to high standards, so as to ensure its reliability. Under
present conditions, without the IDA credit, the EU study would be in a position of needing to respond both
to the long-term needs of the corridor, and to the short-term pressure to fix the road as quickly as possible
and keep the corridor passable. The proposed IDA credit will allow the EU financed study more time to
develop such long-term solutions, including allowing it to better integrate the rail corridor into that solution.
Second, and perhaps most important from a humanitarian point of view, reports are beginning to emerge of
a famine in eastem sections of Ethiopia, on a scale similar to those seen in the 1980's. The immediate
rehabilitation of the Djibouti corridor will be crucial for a sustained relief effort, and the proposed IDA
credit will help ensure that the condition of the road does not become a major hindrance to such efforts.
- 40 -



Costs of the project: While it is customary to include variants of construction methods and pavement
design into the calculus of economic costs and benefits, due to the urgent nature of this project, the project
costs are taken exogenously to the economic analysis. These have been developed separately, as a function
of technical assessment of the condition of the roadway and the construction technique needed to minimize
construction time. The costs used for the purpose of this economic analyis include about $12 million for
rehabilitation of the Dikhil to Galafi portion of the road, $2 million for spot repairs to the Arta spur to
Dikhil section, and about $1.5 million for maintenance and axle-load monitoring over three years. The
remaining $2.5 million in the overall $18 million project involves technical assistance costs which have not
been included in the analysis. Border prices were used in the analysis, which other inputs have been
shadow priced using a standard conversion factor of 0.85.
Results: The results of the economic analysis are shown in Table 1 below. The high IRRs assessed are
common for road maintenance and rehabilitation projects, which generally tend to produce high value.
Factoring the unquantifiable costs identified above would make these IRRs even higher. The low-growth
scenario comparison with continuous grading for 5 years is selected as the most reasonable case. This
results in a project ERR of 31%, and a net present value of $8,666,500 at a discount rate of 10%. Even
under the rather conservative assumptions used for the analysis, the results of the economic analysis shows
that, under any scenario, some kind of intervention is called for.

Methodology
The economic evaluation criteria adopted is the Intemal Rate of Return (IRR) based on economic costs.
Since investment opportunity in Djibouti is rather limited, a discount rate of 10 percent has been used; an
IRR of 10 percent is therefore considered the threshold above which the project is considered economically
worthwhile. The project has been measured against two scenarios of continuous grading and periodic
regravelling of the road instead of rehabilitation, during the interim period until the EU-finance project is
complete (Scenario A and B), and a third minimal action scenario (Scenario C). The minimal action
scenario conservatively assumes that the condition of the road will remain as is that is, it will not
deteriorate further if minimal maintenance is performed on the roadway during the interim period. In
reality, however, the likelihood is that the road would suffer complete breakdown at various times in certain
locations, requiring emergency corrective measures. It is not possible to know the duration or intensity of
these crises, nor what the negative impact on Port operations of such breakdowns would be. It should be
noted, therefore, that the benefits of the project relative to the minimal action scenario are probably
- 41 -



significantly higher even than the high rates of return shown. The project has been assessed for a life of
five years, but a sensitivity scenario has been constructed to test for the effect on the project should the
EU-financed project be fully implemented by in three years (Scenario B).
The World Bank country office for Ethiopia reports that imports into Ethiopia dropped about 40 percent
following the beginning of the conflict with Eritrea. It is unclear what portion of this reduction in traffic
can be attributed to the poor condition of the road from Djibouti; most is probably associated with
economic restraint as a result of the war itself The end of the war would probably bring a surge of imports
as the economy shifts to peacetime activities, but some of these imports might be diverted once again to
Assab, depending on the terms of the peace. Similarly, rehabilitation of the road might also induce traffic
along the roadway, even without an end to the war, if it were assumed that there is significant existing
demand that is suppressed by the condition of the road.
Two traffic growth scenarios have been developed to test the sensitivity of the IRR to these significant
unknown factors. The first assumes no growth on the corridor until the fifth year of the five-year project
life (corresponding roughly to a worst case scenario if the Ethiopian-Eritrean conflict is not resolved.) The
second assumes the pre-war volume of import traffic will resume within four years (by mid 2005), and that
all of it will use Djibouti Port. This translates into a 13.6 percent annual growth rate in traffic beginning in
2001. Annualized 1999 traffic data from Port of Djibouti and the Ethiopian Road Transport Authority
have been used for the base year. In order to be conservative, it has been assumed that traffic is not
increasing along the corridor between 1999 and 2001. None of the scenarios takes into account potential
additional traffic caused by of international famine relief efforts.

Salvage value of investment at            $5.3 million The difference between the initial investment
end of project life                                  and $1.4 million -- representing value of
surface dressing made superfluous by EU
project -- depreciated by 50% (estimate).
The IRR has been calculated based on the stream of quantifiable costs and benefits discussed above. These
include civil works for rehabilitation, maintenance over five years, reductions in vehicle operating costs for
trucking operators, and salvage value at the end of the project life period (five years). Vehicle operating
costs are calculated using the Vehicle Operating Costs module of the World Bank's Highway Design and
Maintenance Model (version III). The inputs to this model are based on Ethiopian rather than Djibouti
economic costs, since these costs are almost entirely paid for within Ethiopia. Because of the nature of the
traffic using the road, only the operating costs to articulated trucks have been taken into account.
Reduction in these costs are primarily the result of a significant reduction in the surface roughness of the
road. The salvage value of the road has been calculated as the difference between the initial value of the
investment in the Dhikil to Galafi section and $1.4 million in surfacing work that would become
economically obsolete if the road were incorporated into the EU-financed project, conservatively
depreciated by 50% for the five year period, to account for its expected value when incorporated in the
future EU-financed project. The other main inputs to the economic analysis, along with justification for
their use, are shown in Table 2 above.
The table of roughness indices used and resulting vehicle operating costs for particular segments, as well as
cash flows for the various scenarios (including sensitivity tests), are shown in the attachment to this annex.
The cash flows conservatively assume that half the credit is disbursed in CY 2000, and half in CY2001.
Likewise, it assumes that benefits to vehicle operators on the roadway begin accruing in July 2001, and
that the year 2001 benefits are only 1/2 what they are in future years. All monetary figures in the cash
flows are in 1999 US dollars.
Sensitivity analysis / Switching values of critical items:
Section F of the Project Appraisal Document identifies several risks which may have the effect of slowing
down implementation of the present project, including risks associated with financial management,
procurement, and Road Department capacity. Sensitivity analysis has been carried out to capture the effect
of the delay of the capital improvement phase of the project for six months (i.e. construction terminates and
benefits begin accruing at the beginning of 2002, rather than mid-2001.) The results are presented below in
Table 3 below.
- 44 -



Table 3. Project IRRs - Effect of Six Month Implementation Delay
Rehabilitate versus ...    High Growth  Low Growth
A. Continuous grading: 5
years                         28%              15%
B. Continuous grading: 3
vears                          1%              -8%
C. Minimal action                 96%             71%
Scenario A: assumes EU-financed project will take 5 years to implement
Scenario B: assumes EU-financed project will take 3 years to implement
Scenario C: assumes present minimal maintenance strategy continues
These results verify quantitatively what intuition suggests, that if the project is delayed in implementation,
the economic value of the project decreases significantly. This decrease in value is so important, that it
would be more economically worthwhile simply to undertake grading for three years rather than to
undertake a delayed rehabilitation were the EU project to be completed early. For the others, however,
while the project would still be economically worthwhile in these instances, the size of the reduction in
IRRs between the basic results and the sensitivity scenario demonstrates the importance of timely
implementation of the project in order to maximize the benefits and economic value of the investment.
T he applicability of the hourly wage rate, calculated on the corridor at $2.35, and independently verified
with the Ethiopian Roads Authority, is also unclear. Drivers on the Addis to Djibouti run are paid by the
day, not the hour. The extent to which changes in travel time due to improvements to the road would
actually affect the wages paid is not straightforward; consequently, it is unclear whether savings in drivers'
hourly wages should be counted among he benefits. Another sensitivity analysis, therefore, has been
carried out to test for the effect of no change in driver wages. These results are shown in Table 4 below.
Table 4. Project IRRs - Effect of Not Including Driver Wage Savings Among Benefits
Rehabilitate versus ...    High Growth  Low Growth
A. Continuous grading: 5          44%             28%
years
B. Continuous grading: 3          23%             10%
vears                   I     _I
C. Minimal Action                127%             96%
Scenario A: assumes EU-financed project will take 5 years to implement
Scenario B: assumes EU-financed project will take 3 years to implement
Scenario C: assumes present minimal maintenance strategy continues
These results show that the all project scenarios are still justified if the wage / time-savings benefits are
dropped from the analysis, at a discount rate of 10%.
Other risk factors are identified in Section F of the PAD, but these relate either to aspects of the technical
assistance or to significant geo-political unknowns, the impacts of which cannot be quantified with any
certainty.

Annex 5: Financial Summary
DJIBOUTI: INTERNATIONAL ROAD CORRIDOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Summary Financial Management Assessment
Three governmental departments are involved in the International Road Corridor Rehabilitation
Project, including the Ministry of Equipment and Transport (MOET), the Roads Department (RD)
(previously within the Ministry of Public Works), and the Road Maintenance Fund (RMF), which was
established in February 2000. MOET and RD are non-revenue earning entities that rely on the
government's budget to fund their operations. RMF is a revenue-earning entity and derives it's revenue by
levying a $15 fee (DF2,700) on loaded Ethiopian trucks entering or leaving Djibouti Port. The levy is
intended to generate funds for maintaining the country's international highways. During the project's
appraisal, an assessment of the financial management systems and procedures at MOET, RD, and RMF
was conducted.
The financial management systems at MOET and RD are subject to Government accounting and budgeting
procedures, which can be complex and time-consuming. Annual budget requests are prepared by MOET
and RD accountants and submitted to MOF for approval. MOET has no formal accounting department
while RD's has limited capacity. Both entities rely, to a large extent, on the Ministry of Finance (MOF) to
process and record payments to contractors and suppliers.
RMF is not a part of the Government's budgeting process and does not receive annual allocations from the
central budget. RMF uses a computerized accounting system to manage its financial transactions, and
employs one full-time accountant to handle all accounting functions. Checks are co-signed by the
accountant and RMF Director. The Director also approves all accounting transactions before being posted
by the accountant.
To ensure adequate financial management of the project, and due to the urgency of implementing the
project, it was decided to establish a stand alone financial management system and procedures, along with
the capacity to manage the system, within the Project Implementation Unit (PIU). The PIU will be in
charge of managing all financial transactions, accounting and reporting activities, including accounting
procedures, internal controls, financial reporting, and auditing arrangements, as well as a Special Account,
which will be opened at the Banque pour le Commerce et l'Industrie. As an interim mechanism, and to
avoid delaying effectiveness, the Project will utilize a manual accounting system until a computerized
system is developed and operational, which is expected to be about five months after effectiveness. The
establishment of a project financial management system, including a manual accounting system, internal
control mechanisms and documented operational policies and procedures, as detailed in the work plan, is a
Condition of Effectiveness. Prior to effectiveness, a Financial Management Specialist, or the external
auditor under the supervision of the Financial Management Specialist, will certify that the financial
management system is satisfactory.
It is anticipated that one accountant will be able to manage the financial transactions associated with the
proposed project. The accountant should have adequate education and experience to handle all accounting
functions and responsibilities, be familiar with International Accounting Standards (IAS), capable of
processing Withdrawal Applications and Replenishment Requests for the Special Account, and be able to
respond to Bank requests for clarification and/or additional information. The appointment of an
Accountant acceptable to the Association will be a Condition of Effectiveness.
- 57 -



A work plan for developing an appropriate financial management system and procedures at the PIU is
outlined below. To ensure that the work plan is implemented on time, immediately after negotiations, a
consultant will begin working with the Ministry of Equipment and Transport to establish a project financial
management system, which will include documented accounting policies/procedures and the budgeting
process, a manual accounting system, internal controls and reporting tools to be used by the PIU. A
Bank-executed PHRD Grant will be used to fund the services of the consultant. Terrns of reference for the
consultant are included in Annex 13.

Audit Arrangements
Project accounts, including the Special Account and SOEs, will be subject to external auditing by a
qualified auditor acceptable to the Bank under agreed terms of reference. The PIU will submit annual
audited financial statements to IDA within three months following the end of the fiscal year; the first such
report will be due March 31, 2001. Due to accelerated expenditures in the project's first year, the auditor's
terms of reference will include the submission of a "management letter" regarding the PIU's operations no
later then six months after effectiveness. This will help to ensure that the PIU's internal controls and
operating procedures are satisfactory. A shortlist of qualified auditors was agreed between the Borrower
and IDA at Negotiations.
- 59 -



Current review of IDA's portfolio in Djibouti indicates that audit reports for most projects are not received
on time. To ensure that audit reports for this project are received on time, a PIU will be established and
staffed with an accountant, and the appointment of a private auditor with terms of reference acceptable to
IDA is a Condition of Effectiveness. Terms of reference for the auditor are included in Annex 13.
- 60 -



Inadeciuate Financial Management Svstem
Part I- Financial Management System:
I have reviewed the financial management system relating to this project. The objective of the review was to
determine whether the project has in place an adequate financial management system as required by IDA
under OP/BP 10.02.
My review, which included visits to the project implementing agencies, was based on the Bank's guidelines
for "Review of Financial Management System", and focused on the assessment of the project's accounting
system, internal control, planning, budgeting and financial reporting system, selection of an auditor as well
as the format and contents of the Project Management Report (PMR) to be submitted by the borrower in
support of Withdrawal Application.
In my opinion, the project does not have satisfy IDA's minimum financial management requirements.
I have detailed in the attachment the inadequacies that I found in the system together with an agreed work
plan by the borrower to remedy the situation.
Signed by:                                             Ayman Abu-Haija, MNSID
Financial Management Specialist                        May 25, 2000
- 61 -



Annex 6: Procurement and Disbursement Arrangements
DJIBOUTI: INTERNATIONAL ROAD CORRIDOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Procurement
Procurement methods (see Table A)
Procurement of goods and works will be in accordance with the World Bank Guidelines for Procurement
under IBRD loans and IDA Credits, published in January 1995, updated in January 1996, August 1996,
and Sept. 1997 and revised in January 1999. The Borrower will use the Bank's Standard Bidding
Documents and standard contracts, as mandated in the Guidelines.
*   Civil works will be procured under National Competitive Bidding (NCB) or carried out by Force
Account, provided no single contract exceeds US$ 3.5 million in which case ICB would be
required. The works to be tendered will be divided into a number of works contracts of appropriate
size (US$ 2-4 million each). All works contracts would be subject to prior review except for
contracts below US$200,000.
*   Goods, including the production of aggregates, will be procured by NCB, except when the contract
value exceeds US$500,000 in which case ICB procedures will be used. Where the goods are of
limited value (less than US$50,000), procurement may be by International Shopping (IS), National
Shopping (NS) or sole source for proprietary parts. All contracts valued at more than
US$200,000 will be subject to prior review as shown in Table B.
Procurement of consulting services will be in accordance with World Bank Guidelines for Selection and
Employment of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers, published in January 1997, revised Sept. 1997 and
January, 1999, and will cover:
3 construction management of the rehabilitation of the international road corridor project;
3 construction supervision of the rehabilitation of the international road corridor project;
3 technical assistance (HIV/AIDS and Road Safety);
3 technical assistance to develop trade related services in Djibouti; and
3   consulting services to staff the Project Implementation Unit.
Thresholds for procurement method and prior review are set out in Table B.
Extensions of existing contracts (where a continuation of the provision of services is appropriate), may be
required on a single source basis, and in such case the terms and conditions of the original contract will
apply, subject to the price adjustment allowed under the contract.

Financial Management Arrangements: Please see Annex 5.
Disbursement Arrangements: The Project will commence with traditional disbursement method. Since
the period of implementation of civil works is only one year, the Project will continue using traditional

Allocation of credit proceeds (see Table C below): The allocation of credit proceeds is given in the
Table C, which also indicates the different financing arrangements for the different categories of
expenditures. Counterpart funds will be made available through in-kind contributions of equipment and
labor. Disbursements against consultancy service contracts will cover 100% of the certified expenditures.
Use of statements of expenses (SOEs): Withdrawals from the loan account may be made on the basis of
Statements of Expenses for the following expenditures:
(a) all operating costs of the PIU;
(b) the services of individual consultants for contracts costing less than US$50,000;
(c) the services of consultants firms for contracts costing less than US$100,000;
(d) training and/or studies; and
(e) goods and works contracts costing less than US$200,000.
The supporting documents for SOE must be maintained by the Project Implementation Unit and then the
Ministry of Equipment and Transport for at least one year after the completion of the Project to allow
review by the external auditor and supervision missions from IDA.
Procurement Reporting. Procurement information will be collected and recorded as follows:
(a) prompt reporting of award information by the Borrower,
(b) comprehensive quarterly reports to the Bank by the Borrower (assisted by consultants), indicating:
(i) revised cost estimates for individual contracts and the total project, including best estimates of
allowances for physical and price contingencies;
(ii) revised timning of procurement actions, including advertising, bidding, contract award and
completion time for individual contract;
(iii) compliance with aggregate limits on specific methods of procurement; and
(c ) a completion report by the Borrower within three months of the credit closing date.

Annex 11: Letter of Road Sector Policy
DJIBOUTI: INTERNATIONAL ROAD CORRIDOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
Mr. Inder Sud
Department Director for the Middle East,
Middle East and North Africa Region
The World Bank
RE:   International Road Corridor Rehabilitation Project -
Road Sector Policy
Dear Mr. Sud,
Since mid-1998, Djibouti has been the only port of entry for goods bound for Ethiopia arriving by
sea. Ethiopia's use of Djibouti as the primary port of entry occurred suddenly, as a consequence of the
geo-political situation in the region. Concomitantly, truck traffic on the road corridor between Djibouti
and the Ethiopian border also increased significantly and suddenly, and, as a result, our road infrastructure
has suffered tremendously. This degradation is a concern both to Djibouti and to the region, since further
increases in traffic are expected as famine relief operations for Ethiopia intensify.
The Government of Djibouti recognizes the urgency of improving upgrading this road
connection, and is therefore appreciative of the assistance of the International Development Association
(IDA) in the rehabilitation of the road corridor. This will help secure unhampered and continued road
access from Ethiopia for the next few years, during which time a high quality road connection is
designed and implemented with assistance from the European Union. IDA's assistance is important to us,
since it will help assure the image and competitiveness of Djibouti Port.
The importance of developing the institutional capacity to safeguard the investment being made,
as well as to ensure the longer-term sustainability of the road network, is understood and taken seriously
in Djibouti. To this end, the GD has embarked on a number of institutional and policy reform measures,
being implemented as part of the process to create a more sustainable sector. It is also considering a
range of other institutional measures, for which the input and assistance of IDA would be appreciated.
The purpose of this letter is to apprise you of these implemented and potential institutional developments
and assure you of our commitment to the process. Actions taken and contemplated will help facilitate an
enabling environment for the IDA project.
The actions which have been taken or are underway include:
(a)    Establishment of a Road Fund. A Road Fund was established on 1st February 2000.
The objective of this fund is to initially finance maintenance of the international corridor
by levying a tariff on trucks leaving Djibouti Port bound for Ethiopia. This funding
source will subsequently be expanded to cover maintenance of the entire national road
network. Under a study financed by the EU the option of outsourcing all maintenance
work to the private sector will be explored, and procedures agreed how this should be
undertaken through a competitive bidding process.
73



(b)    Strengthening of the Administration of Roads. Government is currently considering
different proposals to restructure and reorganize the roads function of the Ministry of
Transport. These proposals include:
(i)   separating the planning and management functions from the force account
operations which previously constituted the bulk of the department's activities;
and
(ii)   commercializing and subsequently privatizing the force account operations.
(c)    Axle load control. Plans are underway to establish overload control facilities in the port
area and eventually to deploy weigh-bridges at strategic locations on the main road
network. We are also in the process of reviewing our axle load and gross vehicle mass
limits with a view to possibly harmonizing them with those prevailing in -he COMESA
region.
In addition to the above, there are a number of other actions that the GD intends to pursue over
the next few years in order to strengthen the institutional capacity of road sector institutions. Many of
these would be undertaken in the context the proposed IDA credit (the Project). These include:
Road Management
(a)    undertake a review of the existing organizational arrangements for management of roads
in Djibouti and define suitable arrangements within the first 12 months of the Project;
(b)   at the Early Implementation Assessment (EIA) of the Project agree on the preferred
organizational model (hereinafter the National Roads Administration - NRA); and
(c)   establish a timetable for creating the NRA and appointing and training the staff in order
to make the NRA fully operational over the remaining three years of the Project.
Road Financing
(a)    undertake a review of possible road user charging instruments during the first 12 months
of the Project, and propose a set of charges that would fully finance the upkeep of the
main road network outside the general budget;
(b)   during the EIA reach an agreement with IDA on a set of charges to be introduced in
phased manner to achieve full funding by June 30, 2004; and
(c)   effect arrangements for depositing the road user charges directly to the account of the
RMF.
Axle Load Limits and Overload Control
(a)   harmonize vehicle weights and dimension limits in conformity with the COMESA region
and have proposed legislation to this effect worked out at the EIA including proposed
fines for overloading; and
74



(b)    strengthen the enforcement of axle load control measures and introduce adequate and
justifiable fines for overloading.
Force Account Operations
(a)    establish a plan within 12 months of the start of the project regarding the role and
administration of force account operations in the road sector in the future, and a review of
the capacity and capabilities of the private sector in undertaking road services.
In conclusion, I would like to reiterate the Djibouti Government's appreciation of IDA's
assistance in this important international corridor project and would also confirm my Government's
commitment to undertake various policy reform measures to safeguard the investment being made in the
international corridor as well as to ensure the longer-term sustainability of the road network.
Yours sincerely,
Osman Idriss Djama
The Minister of Equipment and Transport
75
