DJIBOUTI: INTERNATIONAL ROAD CORRIDOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
INSTITUTIONAL DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM FOR THE ROAD SECTOR
Terms of Reference
A.  BACKGROUND
1.    The Government of Djibouti (GD) has requested assistance from the World Bank, International
Development Association (IDA), to undertake the rehabilitation of the international road corridor between
Djibouti and Ethiopia. This Project (International Road Corridor Rehabilitation Project) includes a
number of components, one of which addresses various policy, institutional and human resource issues
related to the long-term sustainability of the roads sector.
2.    GD is aware of the need to improve the way in which the roads sector is managed and financed.
A Road Fund was set up in February 2000 but is not yet fully operational and various measures are still
required to be put in place for its effective functioning. In the meanwhile, virtually no maintenance is
being carried out by the Ministry of Equipment and Transport due to lack of funds and inadequate
institutional capacity.
3.    Whilst there is a recognition of the need to improve the way in which the roads sector is managed
and financed, the way forward needs to be carefully considered due to the particular characteristics of the
country which include:
*     a relatively small road network of less than 1200 km;
*     a small population base of less than half a million people;
*     a scarcity of skilled manpower;
*     a very high unemployment rate of about 40 percent; and
*     a largely undeveloped local road contracting industry.
4.    In view of the particular characteristics of the country, traditional approaches to improving the
efficiency and effectiveness of the roads sector as adopted in other countries may not be appropriate in the
Djibouti context. Careful consideration should therefore be given to the manner in which the road sector
is managed. To this end, consultancy services are required to undertake a review of the way the Djibouti
roads sector is organized and managed as a basis for defining its future direction, including the
introduction of effective institutional arrangements and mechanisms for achieving sustainable financing
of the sector.
B.    OBJECTIVES
5.    Based on a thorough analysis of the prevailing institutional and financial arrangements in the
roads sector in Djibouti, the overall objectives of this technical assistance are to:
134



(a)   recommend to GD the most suitable format for undertaking the overall management of
the national road network, considering the existing institutional framework as well as
alternative institutional arrangements with different types and levels of autonomy;
(b)   recommend an appropriate financing mechanism which ensures that maintenance of all
roads is adequately funded, and that sufficient funds are available for rehabilitation,
improvements, new construction and for the administration costs of all road activities;
(c)    prepare an Implementation Plan for putting in place the institutional framework and
financing mechanisms best suited for Djibouti together with an estimate of financial
implications. Such a plan shall be flexible enough to allow a phased introduction of
proposed changes.
C.  SCOPE OF WORK
6.    The TA will propose the establishment of an institutional framework and financing mechanism
best suited for the management of the Djibouti road network in line with the objectives set out in
paragraph 5 above. The study work will focus on two related areas:
*     institutional and related issues - comprised of structures, physical resources,
legislation, powers and policies which together manifest the GD' s responsibilities in this
field; and
financial and cost recovery related issues - embracing the various elements of budgets,
financial planning, sources of revenue and financial management applicable in
Government's resourcing of current and planned road infrastructure commitments,
covering both maintenance and new investments.
Phasing of Work
7.    The Consultant will be required to carry out the assignment in two separate and distinct phases.
*     Phase I will be carried out during the first 12 months of the Project to facilitate a process
of defining the institutional changes necessary and actions required to achieve financial
sustainability of the roads sector with specific recommendations and a time bound
program for their implementation. The proposed changes and the implementation
program will be discussed and agreed with the Government at the Early Implementation
Assessment of the Project to take place at the end of Phase I.
*     Phase II will be carried out during the remaining 3 years of the Project and would
involve (a gradual) introduction of effective institutional and financial arrangements with
the aim of putting in place mechanisms that will achieve full off-budget financing of the
roads sector within a 3 year period.
135



Phase I Activities
Institutional Issues
8.     So that the study may properly address the question of institutional development and change,
consideration must be given to a number of issues which will include but not necessarily be restricted to
the following:
(a)    the adequacy of the current arrangements for managing the road network and the factors
underlying the poor performance of the institutions presently involved in road
management.
(b)    the character, technical and administrative functions of the institutional framework best
suited to undertake overall control of management of the network or any subdivisions of
the network, paying particular attention to various available options including: -
* Reform of the existing institutions
* Creation of an autonomous agency or agencies
* Any other institutional arrangements
9.     For the recommended institutional framework, the study will define: -
*  The specific responsibilities of the institutions;
  The relationship between the institution(s) and the ministries responsible for the
road sector;
*  The mechanism for policy formulation (such as Board of Directors, Advisory
Boards, etc.);
*  Method of selection to ensure the widest representation of stakeholders on the
policy formulation body;
*  Remuneration packages that will enable the recommended institution to attract
and retain the services of suitably qualified professional, technical and skilled
personnel;
*  The administration and allocation of finances available for road related activities
including budget development, management systems, financial accounting
systems, internal auditing, etc.;
*  Human resource development packages which will address the problems
currently faced by the Ministry of Transport in this area, with a view to:
-      improving human resource management
-      improving manpower planning, utilization and career development
-      identifying training needs to enhance on the job skills and capacity
* Define External Audit procedures both financial and technical.
136



Financial Issues
10.   The Consultant will consider all options available for financing roads and make recommendations
for their introduction. Financing options may include but not necessarily be limited to Road Tolls, fuel
levies, road vehicle license fees, overload fines, annual budgetary allocations and other alternative road
financing such as Private / Public partnership. Due account will be taken of the existing Road Fund and
its role in any future arrangements for road financing.
11.   The Consultant will also define the powers to be granted to the body(ies) responsible for
managing the Fund as well as specifying the mechanisms to be used in its collection and disbursement.
12.   The Consultant will demonstrate how the Fund will eventually provide sufficient resources to
cover the costs of Development and/or Maintenance of the entire public road network. In order that the
study can recommend sufficient and equitable sources of finance, the following matters will, inter alia,'be
addressed: -
*  The present and future level and structures of costs of a desirable development
and maintenance program for the road system.
*  The cost imposed by different classes of road users on different types of road.
*  The level of use of automotive fuels by road users.
  The revenue required and appropriate levels of contribution to the fund, and
criteria for distribution of the revenue to different categories of roads.
*  The present funding mechanism: the revenue from each source, the mechanisms
and efficiency of collection and whether funds are dedicated to general revenue
or not.
*  Realistic projection of future revenue levels under the present arrangements and
any expected shortfall of financing.
*  Potential sources of additional financing and their means of collection.
*  The advisability of providing a transition period between the present and
proposed revenue levels, including the possibility of using external donor finance
to cover remaining shortfall during this period.
13.   The study will consider the fund management's structures and its functions, addressing the
following issues amongst any others which may be appropriate:
*  Co-ordination between the Fund Management and those Ministries and other
public and private sector organizations, which are involved in the road sector.
*  The location, composition and method of appointment of the Fund management
body, bearing in mind its relationship with the new institutional framework
proposed under paragraph 4.1 above.
137



* The control of revenue collection and disbursement from the Fund.
* The need to ensure proper transparency through internal and external audit
procedures, together with any measures to prevent fraud and irregularities.
*  The sources of revenue making up the Fund and the means by which they may be
collected and deposited in the Fund.
Statutory Instruments
14.   The proposed institutional framework to manage the national road network and setting up of a
revenue base may require a number of amendments to existing legislation and the introduction of new
statutes. The study will indicate and subsequently draft the necessary amendments to current laws,
including any new statutes that need to be introduced.
Phase II Activities
15.   The Consultant will organize the Early Implementation Assessment workshop and make
necessary presentations and participate in the discussions to clarify issues, options and
proposals/recommendations. Phase II activities will focus on the implementation of the various Phase I
recommendations agreed with GD at the EIA workshop. Such implementation will be carried out during
the remaining 3 years of the Project.
16.   During the EIA workshop, the Consultant will propose an appropriate management system for the
transition process itself, utilizing any resources that may be available locally.
17.   The Consultant will set out a timetable mapping the transitional stages during which the existing
management arrangements for the road network will be transformed into the new institutional framework.
Proper account will be taken of the lead times likely to prevail in filling key staff positions and the delays,
which can sometimes take place during the legislative process.
18.   The Consultant will identify elements of the proposed reforms, which can be implemented in the
short term within the current institutional arrangements in advance of the full implementation of the
agreed recommendations emanating from the EIA.
Conduct of the Study Work
19.   The study work will be conducted as a fully participatory exercise. The Government will appoint
a Reference Group that will guide the Consultants and ensure that the study in its analyses and
recommendations is aiming at sector reforms and future institutional frameworks, which are compatible
with Government policy.
20.   The study will establish and maintain the closest possible consultations with the key Government
Ministries, Departments and agencies concerned in the matter, local Government organizations, and the
organizations representing the main stakeholders in the private sector, and will ensure appropriate liaison
with representatives of the donor community currently supporting road sector activities.
21.   During the execution of the study phases, ample time will be allowed for discussions,
consolidation of findings, and planning of the next steps. The consultations are to be facilitated by a
liberal distribution of the study progress reports, after each phase.
138



22.   The study will review the experience of other countries in Africa and elsewhere, in particular for
countries which share similar historical and developmental backgrounds and which may have embarked
on the same kind of exercise with regard to reform of management and financing of their road networks.
The Consultants will prepare an overview of developments and experience elsewhere as part of the Phase
I tasks, and prepare a study tour for key policy makers as part of the Phase I activities.
23.   It is expected that the Consultant will establish a project office in Djibouti for the duration of the
Study, and that all report preparations will be made through this office.
Study Team Composition
General
24.   The study team should incorporate individuals with a wide range of proven professional
experience in a number of disciplines including but not limited to those listed below:
Institutional Reform and Management
25.   The expert(s) will ideally have a background of senior position(s) in public and/or private
enterprises, experience from institutional and management reforms, and the introduction and gainful use
of modern management systems and tools. The expert(s) will have good academic qualifications in
business administration or equivalent, and proven experience in dealing with human resource
development and staff rationalization.
Road Sector Planning and Works Programming and Execution
26.   The expert(s) will have extensive experience of planning, programming and execution of road
maintenance and rehabilitation in developing countries, including use of road agencies own resources and
Consultants / contractors, and from use of equipment as well as labor based technologies. The expert(s)
will be familiar with the issues associated with government held stocks of plant and equipment, and have
an understanding of the economics of highway related activities and the use of computer based highway
investment and maintenance management models.
Financing Systems Design and Operation
27.   The expert(s) will have experience from banking and/or the financial echelons of public or private
corporations, and a clear appreciation of technical and financial audit methods and procedures. The
expert(s) will have relevant academic and professional qualifications and be famniliar with financial issues
and problems in developing countries.
Djibouti Law and Legal Systems
28.   The expert(s) will be completely familiar with the parliamentary and legal system in Djibouti and
of the legal implications and opportunities of public sector reformns including decentralization and
privatization services.
Study Management
29.   The study team leader will be responsible for the overall management of the study progress, of
the study resources, for liaison and co-operation with the Steering Committee, and the necessary
139



communication with senior personnel in government ministries and public and private organizations. The
team leader should have the capacity to motivate both the study team itself and the Steering Comrnmittee,
and to engage the sector organizations to be affected by the implementation of reform proposals in a
fruitful dialogue. This will call for a considerable degree of diplomacy coupled with a consistent
advocacy of the study objectives.
30.   It is stressed that the above guidelines outline required professional competence and experience
and not specific team positions. Individual experts may provide part of the requirements within a
discipline, or may cover parts of or the totality of several disciplines.
31.   It is expected that the study team will include two or more senior experts including the team
leader, who will stay with the study for its duration. It is further expected that the study team will include
a number of experts who have good knowledge of Djibouti socio-economic conditions and culture, and of
the structures and characteristics of the Djibouti public and private sectors.

General

the Project Implementation Unit (PIU). The Consultant will report to the Director of the PIU or his
representative.
Information Availability
33.    The client will make available to the Consultant any documentation that is currently obtainable
from organizations and has relevance to the study. Otherwise the Consultant will be required to obtain all
relevant information or reports related to the study.
34.    Relatively little up-to-date information is available on the road network in terms of quantitative road
conditions, traffic flows, maintenance costs, etc. As a result, the Consultant will be required to verify
existing data and to obtain new data pertinent to the requirements of the $tudy.
35.    Some documentation is available which relates to institutional, management and organization
arrangements including proposals for a number of potential alterations to existing arrangements. This will
serve as a starting point, but thereafter, the Consultants appointed will need to consider this information in
the broader context of the requirements of the objectives of the Study given in paragraph 3.
36.    The Consultant will make an inventory of any documents received and will return these to the
Client not later than the time of delivery of the final report.

representatives from various stakeholder organizations, to monitor progress of the Consultants and
resolve questions that arise during the course of the study.

Consultant on all matters related to the execution of the study. The liaison Officer will advise and
facilitate contacts with other relevant organizations for the Consultant in necessary.

DJIBOUTI: INTERNATIONAL ROAD CORRIDOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
HIVIAIDS STUDY TO DEVELOP A WARENESS AND AN OUTREACH PROGRAM
Terms of Reference
The consultants shall carry out the following reviews and analyses:
(a)   Identify groups which are vulnerable to HIV/AIDS infection along the Djibouti-Galafi
corridor.
(b)    Review existing HIV/AIDS activities in Djibouti, including activities implemented and/or
programmed by the Ministry of Health under financing from the World Health
Organization. Identify funding or other resource gaps, particularly for activities
impacting the Djibouti-Galafi road corridor.
(c)    Supplement existing HIV/AIDS education programs in Djibouti (for example, those
supported by the WHO) by identifying specific interventions applicable to the transport
sector in general, and the Djibouti-Addis corridor in particular, if these have not been
identified previously. Where particular obstacles to such programs are evident, such as
language barriers, propose targeted activities to address such obstacles. Identify
supplemental educational activities for emergency services personnel, and first aid
training of police and highway personnel. The proposed interventions would be
implemented during Phase II of project implementation (years 2 to 5).
(d)    Working with WHO and the Ministry of Health, identify the principal gaps in knowledge
about HIV infection in Djibouti, particularly along the Djibouti-Galafi corridor. Identify
the steps that would be necessary to rectify such a gap, and develop appropriate sample
Terms of Reference for such a study.
(e)    Assist in identifying co-financing for an HIV/AIDS-related intervention during Phase II
of the Djibouti-rehabilitation of international road corridor project, as well as co-
financing to undertake all or part of the study identified in 0.
(f)    Define action that would mitigate the risk of HIV/AIDS for transport sector workers,
partners, and affected communities and help care for those persons already with AIDS.
(g)    Assist the Ethiopian trucking associations to develop comprehensive HIV/AIDS
prevention and control programs for truck drivers and their families. These programs
might consist of information dissemination, education, training, counseling, peer
education, condom distribution, diagnosis and treatment of STIs and the opportunistic
infections associated with AIDS, as well as medical and social care for infected
employees. Identify what types of facilities along the roadway and at the Port might
assist in the development of these programs.
144



DJIBOUTI: INTERNATIONAL ROAD CORRIDOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
ROAD SAFETY AUDIT FOR NATIONAL ROAD NO. I IN DJIBOUTI
Terms of Reference
The consultants shall carry out the following reviews and analyses:
(a)    Carry out a detailed review of accident black spots, identifying and prioritizing these
according potential accident reduction and cost-effectiveness of possible interventions.
Identify which of these (if any) can be addressed quickly and inexpensively under the
IDA-financed project, and which are more appropriate to be dealt with under the EU-
financed project. This task should be accomplished early in the contract.
(b)    Describe existing traffic safety conditions, as quantitatively as available data allow,
inventory safety-related laws, regulations and institutions in both Ethiopia and Djibouti,
and identify key problems and issues.
(c)    Propose modifications of existing Djibouti laws and regulations concerning traffic and
vehicle safety, vehicle registration and inspection, and driver licensing to achieve greater
uniformiity with international regulations like the Vienna Convention or the North-
American Commercial Vehicle Safety Alliance (CVSA), and identify what changes to
Ethiopian laws might be necessary to ensure compatibility between the two countries.
(d)    Work with representatives of the Port of Djibouti, the various Ethiopian trucking
associations and the Roads Directorate, to help develop an emergency-response brigade
along the corridor which can quickly respond to traffic accidents along the corridor, in
terms of providing medical assistance to accident victims, traffic re-stabilization
measures, and clean-up assistance in the case of hazardous materials spills.
(e)    Review current data collection practices and design a consistent national traffic accident
data collection system. Define the approach for systematic data analysis (including
identification of black spots) and reporting; select (from among existing packages) a
Road Accident Analysis System and define its gradual implementation on a nation-wide
basis. Propose viable methods of financing such a system. [PERHAPS USE THE
TERM "CORRIDOR" INSTEAD OF "NATIONAL"]
(f)    Develop a detailed action plan to implement, within a 1-2 year period, such a data
collection and analysis system for the road network under the responsibility of the Roads
Directorate.
(g)    Review the current system of vehicle inspections and safety controls, and arrangements
being made for monitoring of axle loading in conjunction with the various Ethiopian
Trucking Associations. Make appropriate recommendations to combine these activities to
develop and efficient, self-regulating system to reduce risk of traffic accidents while
reducing damage to roadways.
(h)    Review the current system of licensing drivers and monitoring their record infractions in
both Ethiopia and Djibouti, including cooperative arrangements between the two
countries. Make appropriate recommendations to make these systems more effective in
reducing traffic accidents.
(i)    Define a strategy to improve traffic safety education along the Djibouti - Galafi corridor.

Annex 15: Review of the Construction Industry in Djibouti
DJIBOUTI:- INTERNATIONAL ROAD CORRIDOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
A.  BACKGROUND
1.    The significant part of the construction industry in Djibouti comprises about six contractors, two
of whom are large foreign contractors, but with a small presence in Djibouti. There was no significant
road work ongoing at the time of the appraisal mission. One contractor, COLAS, had just completed
repair of a part of the international corridor between the Arta turnoff to Dikhil using a French grant; but
the allocated funds were insufficient to repair the entire section. The only significant future road work, in
addition to the Bank's repair of the international corridor, is the reconstruction and possible re-alignment
of the international road by European Union (EU). The project is in the embryonic phase, since the EU
feasibility study team had just mobilized in Djibouti at the same time as the Bank's appraisal mission
arrived. In a recent development, the World Food Programme has proposed infusion of up to $ 4.2
million in funds to perform emergency grading operations on the international corridor. One of the
options being considered is the use of the Roads Department equipment to perform the work.
2.    Because of the paucity of road work in Djibouti, there are no purely dedicated road contractors.
The industry is composed of general contractors, who perform road work; process materials work for the
Port of Djibouti; and work as contractors for developers, performing site work and building work. The
road Djibouti to Galafi was constructed by the French Government in 1976, by COLAS. The French
Government recently expended $2.8 million to use the COLAS firm to repair part of the Arta - Dikhil
road. The reality is that the contractors, currently working in Djibouti, do not have recent experience in
performing work on large projects ( such as the emergency repair of the international corridor) nor does
any one have the resources in the country to execute a large project.
3.    The same condition applies to the Roads Department. There is no recent experience and expertise
in managing large road rehabilitation projects. Most of the recent works have been done under grant
programs, and there are signs of deterioration of recently placed work, suggesting possible deficiencies in
construction, construction supervision, or other areas. In any event, the Roads Department has no current
staff available for supervision or supervisory work. The Roads Department has done no recent
maintenance, probably due to lack of funds. Roads Department personnel were paid their December, 1999
salaries in late April, 2000. However, the department's maintenance unit from Parc/Atelier is building a
parking lot for Djibouti Port trucks at km 12 of the international road.
B.    CONTRACTORS
COLAS: COLAS is a large French company with a presence in fifty countries. The Djibouti
headquarters and equipment yard is located together. In addition to trucks, dozers and rollers, the yard
holds concrete and other equipment needed for building construction. There is one mobile crushing plant
in Djibouti with a capacity of 40 t/hr. That is one-fourth of the capacity needed for the Bank's project. If
required, COLAS would import additional equipment from Madagascar. This would require two months.
COLAS built the international corridor in 1976 and indicated there were two quarries used: one in
Yaboki; and one between Yaboki and Galafi. Plenty of water is available along the road; but wells should
be drilled since 70 cu.m. per day is required to build the road. Water is available in Dikhil and Galafi.
With respect to labor, the cost is high. COLAS imports foremen, operators for the graders, crushing plant
and finishing machines, as well as surveyors. COLAS has a permanent staff of fifty people in Djibouti.
148



COLAS works in partnership with COSMEZZ on some ventures in the country.
COSMEZZ: COSMEZZ has its headquarters in Rome, and is currently constructing the Dry Port
project for the Port of Djibouti. COSMEZZ has a fixed crushing plant in Djibouti with a 350 cu.m. per
day capacity. The plant can be broken down and transferred to another site. The whole relocation and re-
erection process will take two months. COSMEZZ' opinion was that the entire project could be built by
them in 11 months, including two months mobilization and an extra month to bring in equipment from
overseas. The two months initial period is to pursue the contractor's risk of finding the stone and the
water, COSMEZZ thought the work could be placed in six months. COSMEZZ will accept a contract to
furnish aggregates and materials to others, but will not accept materials furnished by others. They too
believed a detour must be allowed in order to complete on time.
COSMEZZ indicated that crushed stone base at the crusher in Djibouti City cost about $12/cu.m. Skilled
workers are paid $3.60 to $4 per hour, and unskilled workers $0.80 to $0.90 per hour. 19.5% must be
added for taxes. A hidden cost is that different workers must be used in different regions, or tribal limits.
The Dikhil - Galafi road crosses three such regions.
CONCORDE: CONCORDE is a local Djibouti company started in 1979, and specializes in the
construction of roads. They are currently working in joint venture with COSMEZZ and are working with
COLAS on the international corridor. Concorde is completing bridge construction for the French army
and is now starting a port job. They do buildings as well. Equipment owned includes graders, vibratory
and rubber tired rollers, a bitumen distributor and a Belgian rock crusher rated at 300 to 400 cu.m./day.
Internal rental rates are charged for company owned equipment.
Concorde has perforned crushed stone base work in the past. In Djibouti City, crushed stone base costs
4500 DF ($25.42)/cu.m. delivered, and 5000DF ($28.25) /cu.m. outside for a 15 km haul. Truck drivers
earn 100,000 to 110,000 DF per month (approximately $650). There are local social charges. Labor costs
are much cheaper in Ethiopia, but there is a national preference for Djiboutians.
In the past Concorde has developed wells between Galafi and Djibouti. Water is eight meters below the
surface. Concorde estimates nine months to build the Bank's project, including mobilization. As a sole
venture their limit of production is 40,000 cu.m. in six months. However, they can work in joint venture.
Concorde has contracts with Salini, an Italian contractor working in Ethiopia and is in a joint venture with
an Iranian company, Cysus for bulk bitumen. Concorde imports spare parts, fuel and bitumen.
VERZI: VERZI started in Ethiopia in 1973 and has been based in Djibouti since 1980. Current work
includes building a 3000 sq.m. workshop at the airport for military equipment and site work. Current
work is 100% private sector, thirty percent of which is site work. Unlike some countries, a foreign
contractor is not obliged to have a local partner in Djibouti.
Verzi's equipment includes:
Concrete batch plant - 100 cu.m. /hr.
Transit mix trucks - 4 @ 8 cu.m.
Concrete pump - 90 cu.m./hr.
Graders - 2
Wheel loaders - 5
Compressors - 6
Dozers - 2 (CAT D8 and D7)
Steel rollers - 3
Excavators 2 (CAT)
149



Hydraulic Backhoe (428)
Dump trucks (16 to 20 cm)- 10
Small spreader for surface treatment
Verzi has no crusher but can mobilize one in 1.5 months. Verzi collaborates with an Italian contractor.
With respect to the Bank project, Verzi is interested in part of the rock crushing and transport. A two
month lead time is required: one month to get the equipment; and one month for erection. With a 150 t/hr.
plant, Verzi could produce 20,000 cu.m. per month. Verzi also could apply surface treatment at the rate of
1 to 1.5 km per day.
Verzi has pipeline experience in the Dikhil - Galafi area. He opined that the biggest problem in Djibouti is
the lack of qualified personnel. Most contractors use Thais and Filipinos.
With respect to Government work, it normally takes the Government three months to award a contract.
Verzi thinks this could be cut to one month with a consultant's help.
FIAN BADEN: FIAN BADEN is a local road contractor whose business includes earthwork, sewage,
building site work and rental of equipment to other contractors. Precast concrete work is also done.
Typical operated equipment rental rates up to 15 days range from 15000 to 20000 DF per hour (177 DF =
1 USD) . Typical rates are: grader - 16000 DF/hour; 950C loader - 15000 to 20000 DF per hour; rollers
and compactors at 10000 to 15000 DF per hour.
Other equipment includes 2 Poclain excavators, including one with a hyrda hammer, (same price as
grader) and trucks.
Production capabilities include foundation installation at 100 cu.m./day; and stone base at 50 to 75cu.m.
per day within 8 km of the plant up to 15 km.
CHIRDON: CHIRDON were not available for an interview but did provide a list of equipment similar
to Fian Baden's.

Annex 16: Review of Road Management and Finance in Djibouti
DJIBOUTI:- INTERNATIONAL ROAD CORRIDOR REHABILITATION PROJECT
1. INTRODUCTION
The strategic importance of Djibouti as a transit center for goods destined for countries in the Horn of
Africa is dependent on an effective transport sector including a good quality road system. Unfortunately,
due to inadequate funding of road maintenance, coupled with increased traffic volumes and axle loads,
many roads have deteriorated prematurely and transport costs are rising. There is therefore an urgent need
to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of the road transport system in order to safeguard the role of
the Djibouti port and the transit corridor in the national economy.
The Government of Djibouti is aware of the need to improve the way in which the roads sector is
managed and financed. A Road Fund was set up in February 1999 but is not yet fully operational and
various measures are still required to be put in place for its effective functioning. In the meanwhile,
virtually no maintenance is being carried out by the Roads Department due to lack of funds and
inadequate institutional capacity.
Whilst there is a recognition of the need to improve the way in which the roads sector is managed and
financed, the way forward needs to be carefully considered due to the particular characteristics of the
country including:
* a relatively small road network of less than 1200 kms;
*  a small population base of less than half a million people;
* a scarcity of skilled manpower;
* a very high unemployment of about 40 percent;
* a largely undeveloped local contracting industry.
Thus, traditional approaches to improving the efficiency and effectiveness of the roads sector as adopted
in other countries may not be appropriate in the Djibouti context. Careful consideration should therefore
be given to the manner in which the road sector is restructured.
2.    DJIBOUTI'S MAIN ROAD NEWTORK
2.1 Network Size and Pavement Type
The total length of Djibouti's national road network is 1171.5 kilometres made up as follows:
[Ra rTpe: (kmn)
[Pve                   _l               ITra*       -       :  Totalr_v-:-c
331.93 (28.3%)      309.4 (26.4%)        530.2 (45.3%)      l 1171.5 (100.0%)
The volume of road upgrading to bituminous standard during the past decade has been limited. Currently,
approximately 28 per cent of the total road length is paved and recently some sections have reverted to
unpaved (e.g. the 102 km section between Dikhil and Galafi) due to a significant increase in traffic
loading coupled with complete lack of maintenance.
151



In addition, there are also some 200 km of urban roads of which 115 km (57.5%) are paved, mostly in and
around Djibouti, and the remaining 85 km (42.5%) are gravel.
2.2 Road Conditions
No current statistics are available on road conditions in Djibouti. However, through donor assistance, a
number of regional (transit) routes have been, or are in the process of being, rehabilitated due to complete
lack of maintenance in the past. This includes approximately 188 km of National Route #1 running
between Arta turnoff through Dikhil to Galafi at the Ethiopian border.
A number of roads have recently been rehabilitated, including the first 33 km from Djibouti towards
Oueah (Arta Turnoff) under an Italian bilateral aid project and a 12 km section between Al Sabieh and the
Ethiopian border at Guelile funded by the Islamic Development Bank. These roads are still in good
condition but, unless they are adequately maintained in future, they will suffer premature deterioration
and require to be upgraded as before (a vicious cycle of construct-no maintenance-reconstruct).
2.3  Road Traffic
Long-distance traffic is heaviest on the route from Djibouti to Ethiopia via Galafi which is currently
carrying some 400 - 500, 5-6 axle commercial vehicles per day in one direction at the Galafi end. Almost
all this traffic emanates from the port of Djibouti which currently serves as the entry point for imports to
Ethiopia. This traffic is expected to increase significantly in the short to medium term due to famine relief
operations for Ethiopia.
Traffic on the most heavily loaded links in Djibouti city is currently averaging over 15,000 vpd, but these
volumes drop considerably outside of the city.
3.  MANAGEMENT OF THE ROAD NETWORK
3.1    Organization and Responsibilities
In the past, management of the national road network was the responsibility of the Public Works arm of
the Ministry of Public Works, Housing and Urban Development, which was adequately staffed with a
clear mandate. Since the transfer of the road function of the Public Works Department to the Ministry of
Equipment and Transport in 1999, there has been a considerable reduction in qualified staff, the manager
of the department has been appointed on a temporary basis for more than a year, and the role and mandate
of the department has become unclear. Five engineers and two technicians have been transferred from the
Roads Department to other ministries within Government. This loss of staff has significantly weakened
the institutional capacity of the department to operate in a satisfactory manner.
The staffing of the Roads Department is currently as follows:
2000:       1999:
No. of Engineers                                                       3         8
No. of technical advisors (French)                                     1         1
No. of Technicians                                                     5         7
No. of administrative, accounting, clerical, etc staff               281       420?
152



There is also a force account unit comprising some 300 personnel and an equipment fleet stock of about
160 pieces of all types of road construction and maintenance equipment. This unit falls under the Roads
Department and its main function is to carry out public works within Government. It is often directed in
its operations by the Ministry of Equipment and Transport. A government owned soils testing laboratory
for buildings and public works, the LBTP, also falls under the Ministry of Equipment and Transport, is
staffed with civil servants, and provides services on a commercial basis to both the public and private
sector.
3.2    Road Maintenance Funding
The available road budget and that actually utilized on salaries and road works during the past 5 years is
summarized in the following table:

As is apparent from the above table, the amount budgeted to the Roads Department over the past 5 years
has declined substantially by about 53%. Between 1996 and 1998, approximately 75% of the budget
made available to the department has been utilized for paying staff salaries, a figure which reduced to
58% in 1999. In 1998 and 1999 the funding received was insufficient to fully pay staff salaries, while up
to March 2000 no salaries have yet been paid since November 1999. Not surprisingly, staff morale is low
and, due to lack of funding, practically no maintenance is being carried out on the main road network,
resulting in deteriorating road conditions.
3.3 Impediments to Effective Road Management
The impediments to effective road management in Djibouti are numerous and include the following:
(a)    inadequate skills and capacities in the road administration;
(b)    lack of a clear organizational structure and responsibilities;
(c)    insufficient funds for all aspects of road management including maintenance and
rehabilitation;
(d)    inadequate planning, programming and budgeting of road works;
(e)    lack of adherence to agreed budgets and little accountability for performance;
(f)    inadequate systems for the effective monitoring and auditing of expenditure and
payments; and
(g)    weakness in procedures for the disbursement of funds.
Under the present arrangements, there seems to be little likelihood that the above impediments will be
redressed.
153



4.   ATTEMPTS AT ROAD SECTOR RESTRUCTURING
Government's recognition of the need to reform the roads sector has led to the establishment of a Road
Fund for off budget funding of road maintenance. There are also plans for re-organizing and
strengthening the Roads Department, introducing a better system for road planning and budgeting, and
introducing axle load control measures.
4.1   Establishment of a Road Fund
By Decree No. 1440 dated 15' August 1999, a Road Maintenance Fund (RMF) was created on 1st
February 2000 to finance the maintenance of the national road network, with priority to be given to the
regional (transit) routes. The funding sources will subsequently be expanded to cover maintenance
funding of the entire national road network.
The RMF currently has a staff complement of 16 people and has recently established its own offices,
accounting system, etc. The monthly revenue is currently of the order FDB 15 - 20 million. The
Management Committee (5 member Board with majority from private sector) is scheduled to meet every
3 months but is yet to have its first meeting.
The main concerns center on the way in which the Road Fund is currently constituted, in that there is no
provision for separate planning, programming and budgeting by the MOET/Roads Department to provide
a rational basis on which the road fund can disburse funds, and determine the level of charges needed to
fully fund maintenance of the road network. Given this shortcoming, there is a significant risk that the
RMF would develop these functions in house, in which case there could be inadequate controls and
conflict of interest if the oversight function of the Board is weak. Alternatively, there is a risk that the
funds could be diverted for purposes other than road maintenance.
The main features of the fund are given in Attachment A.
4.2 Proposed Strengthening of the Roads Department
Proposals have been prepared recently by the Ministry of Equipment and Transport as well as the Roads
Department to restructure and reorganize the Roads Department. The proposals envisage that:
(a)   there will be a separation of the planning and management functions of the Roads
Department from the force account operations which previously constituted the bulk of
the department's activities;
(b)   the Roads Department's primary responsibilities in future would focus on road planning,
programmning and budgeting whilst execution of works would be undertaken by
competitive bidding in the private sector;
(c)   in fulfilling its new mandate, the Roads Department is planned to be strengthened and
subsequently or immediately transformed into an autonomous Road Authority; and
(d)   the force account unit would eventually be privatized and in the interim should operate
along commercialized lines.
4.3 Introduction of Road Management System
154



The French Government has agreed in principle to assist the Roads Department with the introduction of
an appropriate Road Management System for better planning, programming and budgeting activities
using an economic model such as the World Bank's HDM-III/IV. However, this initiative has been put on
hold pending the re-organization of the Roads Department and the establishment of a more sustainable
structure better able to make proper use of the Road Management System.
4.4 Introduction of Axle Load Control
Currently, there is no overload control in Djibouti. The result is that overloading is undoubtedly
occurring, the extent and consequence of which is likely be significant in terms of accelerated road
deterioration and increased maintenance needs.
Plans are underway to establish overload control facilities in the port area and eventually to deploy weigh-
bridges at strategic locations on the main road network. The introduction of these facilities needs to be
accelerated and appropriate, economically based fines introduced. Careful consideration should be given
to the management of the weigh-bridge operations with options including the use of the private sector on
a performance management basis or possibly under the Roads Department. Safety considerations also
need to be effectively addressed and closely monitored.
5.   ROAD MAINTENANCE
5.1 Funding: Requirements versus Appropriations
Maintenance of the road network has consistently fallen behind requirements and, since independence,
successive Governments have been unable to adequately fund road maintenance activities. Until very
recently with the establishment of the Road Fund, the sole source of funding for road maintenance has
been Government budgetary allocations. Based on updated estimates prepared by consultants, the annual
maintenance needs are estimated as follows:
* 203 million FDJ for urban roads
*  676 million FDJ for the national road network (about US$4 m), of which:
* 352 million FDJ for roads of regional (transit) importance,
- 324 million FDJ for the remainder of the road network.
From the above estimates, it is apparent that over the past five years at least, the funds released to the
Roads Department for maintaining national roads (average 16 million FDJ) was less than less 3 per cent
of the estimated annual requirement!
5.2 The Consequences of Inadequate Maintenance
As a result of past neglect, the paved road network has deteriorated badly and many sections have had to
be rehabilitated at a much higher cost than timely maintenance. An example is the urgent need to restore
the National Route #1 between Arta turnoff-Dikhil-Galafi, to a stable, maintainable state. This will
involve extensive upgrading and rehabilitation for which donor assistance has had to be sought. The
ultimate success of such assistance will depend on the country's ability to fully fund future road
maintenance on a sustainable basis and to manage the sector effectively.
Another consequence of inadequate maintenance is the higher vehicle operating costs incurred by the
transporters resulting in higher consumer prices.
155



5.3 Axle Load Limits
Djibouti' s axle load limits are similar to those of other Francophone countries. The single axle load limit
on the rear axle is 13 tonnes compared with 10 tonnes in most Anglophone countries, including Ethiopia.
Thus, as a result of the 4t power law which relates axle load to pavement damage, the damaging effect of
such axles is relatively very high. Djibouti needs to decide on what are appropriate axle load limits,
taking account of developments in the COMESA region, of which it is a member, as well as its bi-lateral
agreement with countries such as Ethiopia, Eritrea and Somalia.
6. CONCLUSIONS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
Against the above observations, and in the context of this emergency rehabilitation project, the following
are some conclusions that can be drawn regarding financing and management of roads in Djibouti:
(a)    The poor condition of the road network is seriously affecting the reliability of the road
corridor which in turn is jeopardizing the role of Djibouti as entry port for the region;
(b)    Past neglect of maintenance has resulted in loss of road assets, the restoration of which
will require substantial funding in the future;
(c)    Reliance on annual budgetary appropriations for road maintenance is unsatisfactory as
allocations in the past have been grossly inadequate;
(d)    The prevailing high axle load limits, high degree of overloading and lack of enforcement
of legal limits are compounding the adverse effects of lack of maintenance;
(e)    The introduction of a Road Fund has provided a good start in securing a sustainable
source of funding for road maintenance independent of the regular budget. Additional
funding is required to fund the maintenance of the entire road network, and should now
be given priority.
(f)    To manage the sector more efficiently and effectively the institutional arrangements
should now be reviewed urgently and an action plan agreed to achieve this objective as
soon as possible.
RECOMMENDED PLAN OF ACTION
(a)    An framework agreement to be reached between the GD and IDA on the future direction
of the road sector, including management and financing, as part of the preparation of this
project, with concrete steps and a time bound program;
(b)    Undertake agreed studies, workshops and other necessary actions during the first 12
months of the Project to facilitate a process of achieving institutional transformation and
financial sustainability of the road sector;
(c)    Agree with the GD at an Early Implementation Assessment of the Project on a three year
action program to introduce effective institutional arrangements and full off budget
financing of the road sector;
(d)   Implement this three year program as a component of the Project.
156



(e)    The future role of force account operations in the road sector should form part of the
studies, and be discussed and decided upon at the EIA.
Terms of Reference for the first year activities are given in Annex 13.
157



Attachment A
Review of Road Management and Finance in Djibouti
Main Features of the Djibouti Road Fund
(a)    Revenue is derived from charging all trucks using Djibouti's transit routes, as well as
those going to Lake Assal, a levy of US$ 0.5 per tonne up to a maximum of US$15;
(b)    The revenue is collected by the Fund accountant and deposited in an account at the
National Bank of Djibouti;
(c)    The receipts from the account are to be used to fund road maintenance only by
competitive bidding in the private sector. New construction will continue to be financed
under existing budgetary procedures;
(d)    The receipts, expenditures, accounting and management of the Fund must follow normal
budgetary and auditing rules and procedures; and
(e)    The Fund is to be administered by a management committee comprised of the following
five members:
(i)    A representative of the Ministry of Equipment and Transport;
(ii)   A representative of the Ministry of Finance;
(iii)   A representative of the Chamber of Commerce and Industry; and
(iv)   Two representatives from the transport industry (one each from Djibouti and
Ethiopia).
The committee will choose its president from among its members and will take decisions by simple
majority;
(a)    The administration of the fund will be carried out by an administrator who will be
charged with:
(i)   carrying out the decisions taken by the management committee;
(ii)   preparing and proposing the budget of the Fund; and
(iii)  representing the fund in legal matters.
(b)    A Fund accountant nominated by the Minister of Finance will be responsible for:
(i)   ensuring the procedures of the Fund are in conformity with public accounting
procedures;
(ii)   carrying all accounting and financial operations of the Fund; and
(iii)  producing periodic reports of the status of the Fund to the Treasury.
158



(c)    All maintenance programs and expenses must receive the prior approval of the
Management Committee

Attachment B
Review of Road Management and Finance in Djibouti
COMMERCIALIZATION OF THE ROAD SECTOR
Support for the Fee-for-Service Principle
There is a recognition in Government that alternative funding sources, other than relying on annual
budget appropriations, need to be developed and adopted. Indeed, the establishment of the Road Fund on
1S" February 2000 is a step in that direction. Moreover, proposals from the previous and current Minister
of Transport support the principle of road maintenance cost recovery from road users. However, the
approach appears to be a gradualist one, which has started with a Road Fund that initially focuses on
charges on transit traffic only. This principle is proposed to be extended to cover all roads and to possibly
have two road funds, one for urban roads and one for other national roads.
Realization and Implementation of Policy Proposals
Notwithstanding the proposals made in the past to restructure the management and financing of roads in
Djibouti, these proposals need to be transformed into practical achievement. This will require strict
adherence to the principles of effective governance in the operation of a system of road user charges. If
the problems of the past are to be redressed as regards lack of effective maintenance to preserve
substantial investments made mostly by donors in rehabilitating existing roads, then there must be an
assurance that sooner rather than later a comprehensive system of cost recovery in the roads sector is
established, together with the institutional mechanisms required to ensure the transparent and effective
management of the funds generated, and their guaranteed use on road maintenance programs.
Cost Recovery Options
Government needs to consider several different ways of recovering road maintenance costs from road
users, together with several levels of cost recovery. The choice will depend on practical considerations -
the respective roles of national and municipal authorities, the likely response of users, the reforms in road
management that are realistic - as much as the costs that are attributed to different road users and their
link with charging mechanisms.
In general, the system of road user charges that should be considered will aim:
(a)   to make road users face the economic consequences of their use of roads, with charges
reflecting the costs they cause to the network; and
(b)   to establish a source of finance that will allow road operations and maintenance to be
funded on a sustainable basis.
The Link Between User Charges and Better Roads
In designing the cost recovery system, the Government should endeavor to create a link between what
road users are willing to pay, what is spent on roads, and the quality of roads that results. By this way a
quasi-market would encourage users to demand value-for-money and impose budget discipline on the
road agency.
Other components of the system would be:
160



(a)    A more commercially-minded, customer-oriented road authority that is both responsive
and accountable to road users for the efficiency of its spending and the resulting quality
of the road network;
(b)    as much competition as possible among suppliers of road construction and maintenance
services inputs, to reduce their costs; and
(c)    a system of planning, supervision and monitoring which gives users and other
stakeholders - not just the road authority and Government - a role in influencing road
spending levels and priorities.
Existing Road user Charges, Fees and Taxes
Total Government receipts from road users for ownership and use of vehicles (import taxes on vehicles,
and parts, stamp duty, insurance tax, motor fuel and lubricants tax, etc) are estimated by the Ministry of
Equipment and Transport at 2 to 2.5 billion FDJ annually. Thus, road users already pay a substantial
amount of various taxes which significantly exceeds maintenance requirements. This raises important
practical questions about how revenue from road users should be raised in future - by imposing additional
levies or ring-fencing a portion of the various taxes for a dedicated road fund. In other countries where
road users contribute relatively little towards road maintenance funding, dedicated road funds have been
established. Where road users already contribute significantly to road maintenance funding, a portion of
the taxes paid can be ring-fenced for this purpose.
MANAGEMENT AND ADMINISTRATION OF MAINTENANCE FINANCES
Ensuring Effective Road Maintenance
There should be mechanisms in place to ensure that revenue collected from road users result in an
efficient, optimal program of maintenance. Users will not be willing to incur cost-recovery charges. if
better roads do not result. This will require the establishment of a simple Road Management System,
which will allow a rational, needs-based maintenance plan to be produced. In addition, it will be
necessary to put in place the following:
(a)    cast-iron procedures for efficient management of funds collected from user charges, and
for protecting them from alternative budget claims;
(b)    an agreed, rational basis for allocating the proceeds among maintenance works, based on
a regularly updated annual maintenance plan which maximizes economic return from
expenditure;
(c)    a capable, efficient and fully accountable road authority which is able to draw on a
competitive contracting industry for cost-effective inputs;
(d)   systems and procedures for monitoring and evaluating the effectiveness of maintenance
operations in terms of their impacts not just on road conditions but also on user costs;
and, most importantly; and
(e)    a system of supervision and control which allows user representatives to have a say in
how much should be spent on road maintenance and the level of charges needed to
finance it.
161



Other measures to ensure the effective road maintenance include:
(a)    private sector participation in the management and auditing of the funds derived from
road user charges;
(b)    alternative arrangements for the delivery of road maintenance services, including
possibly the establishment of a commercialized road authority with greater responsibility
- and accountability - for the performance of the network; and
(c)    strict controls over the use of maintenance funds, including reporting requirements that
include information on the effectiveness of road maintenance in reducing user costs and
the value for money derived from road user charges.
162

Major Project Components:
The project would include the following main activities:
(a) a low cost temporary improvement of National Road #1, between Dikhil and Galafi, and subsequent
maintenance of this section for 3-4 years;(b) repairs of National Road #1, between the turnoff to Arta and
Dikhil (88 kms), and routine maintenance for 3-4 years after improvements and repair works have been
undertaken; (c) procurement of equipment for axle load control and to make plant pool operational; and
(d) technical assistance. The TA component would facilitate development of trade related services in
Djibouti, assist the Roads Department in undertaking the project within a very short time frame and
include a road safety program. Project will include an HIV/AIDS education and outreach program. If
practical, the project would also implement agreed policy and institutional recommendations of an EU
financed study and undertake various other studies to be agreed upon.

Major Environmental Issues: (identified or suspected in project)
No major environmental issues are anticipated for this project, since it represents rehabilitation of an
existing road only, with no changes in the alignment. There are some minor impacts associated with
repair and upgrading of the road, including: removal and disposal of waste materials from road bed and
disposal of containers of bitumen and other types of sealers; risk of localized pollution of land and soils
from disposal of tires, broken parts, waste fuel, and used lubricants for heavy machinery and trucks
engaged in construction; temporary aggravation of noise and dust levels during construction; and
potential damage to local vegetation by provision of temporary bypass roads during construction. It is not



2
EDS
anticipated that induced demand will be a significant short-term factor from an economic or
environmental perspective since the road has historically had very little passenger travel, and the freight
traffic between Djibouti and Addis (predominantly petroleum products, fertilizer, and food aid) is highly
inelastic to either travel time or cost.
Other Environmental Issues: (of lesser scope associated with project)
The proposed project does not include involuntary resettlement or land acquisition since all construction
will be within the existing road alignments and construction materials will be taken from previously
developed sites which are government owned. The project area does not include any protected areas or
areas of critical natural habitat. It is not anticipated that any archaeological, historical or sacred sites
would be affected by the proposed project.
Proposed Actions: (to mitigate environmental issues described above)
An Environmental Management Plan (EMP) has been prepared for the proposed project that includes
specific measures to mitigate potential construction and operation period impacts and to address road
safety issues. Key activities in the EMP would include: contract requirements for proper management of
construction waste; control measures for waste fuel, oil and lubricants; reduction of noise and dust levels;
and rehabilitation of areas used for bypass roads and construction materials. The plan includes measures
for improvement and monitoring of road safety. Provisions have also been made for use of "chance find
procedures" for buried or unrecorded archaeological and historical sites. The EMP has been prepared in
cooperation with the Ministry of Habitat, Urban Development, Environment and Territorial Planning
(MOE) and the Higher Institute for Scientific and Technical Studies and Research (ISERST) which is
responsible for cultural heritage.
Justification/Rationale for Environmental Category: (presents reasons for environmental category selected
and explanation of any changes from initial classification, including whether any changes relate to alternatives)
The environmental category is justified, since the project represents rehabilitation of an existing road
only, with no changes in the alignment. The potential impacts of project supported activities are well
known and can be effectively addressed through engineering design, low cost mitigation measures and
proper construction monitoring. Construction contracts will include specific environmental provisions
and contractors will receive a briefing concerning these requirements. An archaeologist from ISERST
will conduct on-site inspections during major earth moving and materials collection activities and will
monitor the use of archaeological "chance find" procedures by contractors.
Reporting Schedule: (Category A EA report: Start-up date, date for first draft, and current status. Category B: Is
there a separate environmental analysis? If yes,when is it due?)
Start-up date February 2000
Date for first draft April 2000
Current status May 2000 - Environmental Management Plan Completed
Remarks: (gives status of any other environmental studies, lists local groups and local NGOs consulted and,
where EA reports were made public locally, tells whether borrower has given permission to release EA report, etc.)
A field based review was conducted by Bank environmental staff in February 2000 with the participation
of senior MOE representatives. Addidtional site visits were made by Bank and MOE staff in April and
May 2000. This included site visits to all areas proposed for improvements under the project. The visit



3
EDS
confirmed that the potential environmental impacts associated with the proposed road rehabilitation
activities would be limited in scope and duration. Consultations with stakeholders from national and
local government, embassies and international organizations, applied research institutes, private sector
and nongovernmental organizations have been conducted in Djibouti City and in the project area. The
Borrower has made the Environmental Management Plan available in English and French within Djibouti
and it has been made available through the InfoShop.
